Ihe Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
e^e re i ects as uninteresting more
ar J a °r w hat appears in the‘newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin. -- ,
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest: The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains v
and has become the representative Australian*
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly -spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or !ess endeavoured to profit by its exaMple
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing ’
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
hrst issue of The Bulletin. v
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had, at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. 1 hey are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success iti
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility On the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to .the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the'cla l ms of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised,"' The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared bv its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of ail Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bus liman of the Far North?, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

The Faroo "River.”
(Accent on the Par. Inverted commas o'er the River,
(FOR THE BULLETIN).
It Was a week from Christmas time,
As near as I remember,
And half a year since in the rear
We’d left the “Darling-Timber.”
The track was hot and more than drear—
The long day seemed forever—
Hu't now we knew that we were near
Our camp—the Paroo River.
With blighted eyes and blistered feet,
With spirits out of order—
Half mad with flies and dust and heat
We’d crossed 1 the Queensland Border.
I longed to hear a stream go by
And see the circles quiver j
I longed to lay me down and die
That night on Paroo River.
’Tis said the land out West is grand—
I do not care who says it —
It isn’t even decent scrub,
Nor yet an honest desert;
It’s plagued with flies, it’s broiling holv
A curse is on it ever ;
I really think that God forgot
The country round that river.
My mate —a native of the land —
In fiery speech and vulgar.
Condemned the flies, and cursed the sand,
And doubly damned the mulga.
He peered ahead, he peered about —
A bushman he, and clever —
“ Now mind you keep a sharp look-out;
We must be near the river.”
The “nose-bags” heavy on each chest
(God bless one kindly squatter!)
With grateful weight our hearts compressed—
We only wanted water.
The sun was setting (in the west)
In colour like a liver—
We’d fondly hoped to camp and rest
That night on Paroo River.
A cloud was on my mate’s broad brow.
And once I heard him mutter :
“I’d like to see the Darling now.
God bless the Grand Old Gutter 1”
And now and then he stopped and said
In tones that made me shiver—
“ It cannot well be on ahead,
I think we've crossed the river. ”
But soon he saw a strip of/ground
That crossed the track we followed —
No barer than the surface round
But just a little hollowed.
His brows assumed a thoughtful frown—
This speech he did deliver :
*■* I wonder if we’d best go down
Or up the blessed river ?”
** Where is the gory, blessed stream ?”
And he replied, “ We’re at it!”
I stood awhile, as in a dream,
“ Great Scott!” I cried, “is that it ?
Why, that is some old bridle-track !”
He chuckled, “ Well, I never !
It’s nearly time you came out-back—
This is the Paroo River!”
No place to camp—no spot of damp—
No moisture to be seen there ;
If e’er there was it left no sign
That it had ever been there.
But ere the morn, with heart and soul
We’d cause to thank the Giver—
We found a mouldy water-hole
Some ten miles down the river.
HENRY LAWSON.
“Lord Rosebery promises to become a
sort of superior music-hall favourite. He is very
nervous and irritable, and his speech to the party
at the Foreign Office was a manifest failure.”—
Jeames, M.D. (Argus).
“Every civilisation is an aggregation of
Christs in the inverse—not the one dying for the
many, but the many dying for the one. Everyone
of these huge buildings—builded between the
missing of the rope, the breaking of the plank, the
rush of a man in mid-air, and the gathering up of
his fragments by his fellow-martyrs—is a Calvary
—a cross erected by the Syndicate as bloody sacri-
fice to Advertisement, its God.” — Randolph Bed-
ford.
“The mind of every intellectual human
being is part and parcel of the great permanent
mind of humanity; and even if its influence soon
ceases to be traceable —if the spoken words are
forgotten—if the written volume is not reprinted
or even quoted, it has not worked in vain. The
intellectual light of Europe in this century is not
only due to great luminaries whom everyone can
name, bub to millions of thoughtful persons, now
utterly forgotten, who in their time loved the
light, and guarded it, and increased it, and carried
it into many lands, and bequeathed it as a sacred
trust.”— Philip Hamerton’s “ The Intellectual
Life.”
The Yankee inventor literally makes moun-
tains out of molehills. One Brown was watering
his lawn and wanted more pressure. He cub down
a clothesline and wound it round his hose, when
the pressure doubled. That set Brown thinking,
and the result of his thought is the Brown-Seg-
mental wire-gun, which gives a projectile five
times the muzzle-velocity of the best modern
ordnance, and will penetrate the armour of the
heaviest iron-clad. It is made in segments like
the staves of a cask, and the whole transverse
resisting power is given by wire rope wound round
it. Once the air-ship is perfected, however, guns
and armour will be alike useless, since a few cans
of nitro-glycerine casually dropped will destroy an
army or a navy in the twinkling of an eye. In
a very few years this will be accomplished, and
either peace or annihilation must follow.

Dea,r Bullkwn, —Your interesting retros-
pect, “The Victorian Bar,” recalls a scene
between Chief Justice Stawell and Aspinall. A
case was being defended by Aspinall, before a
P'i;y, an d he wound up a rambling speech with :
I do not care whether judges are corrupt, as
long as juries are pure.” A solemn silence ensued,
broken by the indignant remarks of the judge to
the effect that he thought the reflection on the
Bench by the learned counsel was most unjusti-
fiable. He submitted that “ the purity of the
Bench of the Supreme Court of Victoria had
never been questioned, and he protested against
the degrading reference of counsel.” Boor
Aspinall was thunderstrieken—he had not the
remotest idea of having reflected on the Bench,
until a friend enlightened him. Then, in a voice
broken with emotion, and with big tears rolling
down his too-suggestively red cheeks, he blurted
out his apology: “If there was one man in the
world for whom he entertained profound respect
that man was his Honor. He begged he would
forgive the lapsus linyuce , and believe that he was
incapable of intentionally reflecting on the Bench.”
The judge was “visibly affected,” and trusted
the learned counsel would not say another word
about the matter. I think this was the last case
but one in which Aspinall appeared. The end
was fast approaching. A bottle of brandy before
breakfast was producing its inevitable sequel. His
last was a criminal case, and his intellect broke
down in the midst of it. But his sense of respon-
sibility did not leave him. From his bedroom he
wrote an almost incoherent letter to the Argus,
in which he tried to explain certain points in
favour of prisoner that he thought should be made
public. He said the frailties of a lifetime ap-
peared to be crushing him, and he besought the
editor to assist him to make clear his meaning.
The venue-room was changed to the lunatic asylum,
and long after he had left for England, “ Aspinall’s
room ” was shown by the attendants to visitors,
where the strong leather covering of the padding
was torn down like so much paper. He once
defended Dr. Beaney on a charge of wilful
murder, for some alleged malpractice in connection
with a barmaid, who died. The prosecution was
led by Dr. Tracey, the leading doctor of the time.
Aspinall made no defence, and called no evidence.
He simply cross examined the medical witnesses,
theconfiictingcharacterqf whose testimony secured
Beaney’s acquittal. Beaney visited him in his
cell and asked : “Do you remember me ?” “Yes,
you rascal,” replied Aspinall, “I saved your
neck.” He died in England, within a few hours
of the death of his wife in Victoria. She was a
postmistress in a country town. His son is said
bo have been educated by the Duke of Edinburgh,
whose acquaintance Aspinall made on his visiting
Victoria. It was Aspinall who defended the
lunatic O’Farrell, by whom the Duke of
Edinburgh was shot at Sydney, As a con-
sequence of the acceptance by Aspinall of
the brief, the loyal snobs of a certain lead-
ing Sydney club absolutely refused to extend
to the distinguished counsel the usual privilege of
honorary membership during his stay in Sydney.
One exception I would take to the comparisons
between past and present. I think justice has
not been done to Michie, reputedly the greatest
pleader the Australias have as yet produced,
as well as the most widely accomplished. All
sides concede him to have been the greatest
Parliamentary debater, as Higinbotham was
the leading orator. Did not the trial of
Bowie v. Wilson equal in importance, if not
in length, Speight v. Syme? This was a
case of libel brought against the Argus for alleging
that the most revolting cruelty was practised
towards the patients of the Lunatic Asylum, with
the knowledge and approval of Superintendent
Bowie. The disclosures were sensational, and
Michie’s eloquent pleading for the defendant has,
perhaps, never been surpassed. He was a man
who could reply in Parliament in a demolishing
speech without note or pause. Michie’s superior
claims to a Judgeship were indisputable, but his
caustic tongue had been felt and remembered by
those in whose hands lay the gift. In the fifties,
he was proprietor of Melbourne Herald, then
the leading daily paper, Higinbotham being a
contributor. He gave it up <£20,000 in debt, and
promised his creditors to pay them every farthing
—a promise he literally kept. When he was
being brutally hounded down while contesting
St. Kilda, he came to the edge of the platform and
said: “Against the accusations of my calumnia-
tors I place my past life, and if that will not avail
then I have lived in vain!” “The Lion of the
Land League ” never addressed another public
meeting. Higinbotham was defeated by Bent,
through a trick of the Melbourne Club, and
Michie by Snowball. The feeling of the mercan-
tile classes was so bitter against them that both
Michie and Higinbotham were boycotted, and for
18 months—during the tariff struggles—they
never received a brief. — J.S.

THE VALOROUS PARSON’S PORLORN HOPE

“The Apostle of Prohibition.”
THE REV. L. M. ISITT, OF
MAORILAND. ,
THE EIGHT FOR TEMPERANCE.
The Rev. L. M. Isitt, a Maoriland temper-
ance captain, who in recent vigorous campaigna
has earned the titular distinction of The Apostle
of Prohibition,” is in Sydney on his way to the
General Wesleyan Conference about to he held at
Adelaide . . . and in conversation with a
Daily Telegraph reporter, he laid out the history
of the Maoriland temperance movement on a
rapid sketch plan scale -
“ Then you are evidently looking forward to a
big political temperance fight in Maoriland .
“Yes ; at the first opportunity.”
“ Did the women’s vote assist temperance
“Immensely,” Mr. Isitt said, “and from his
own point of view, Mr. Seddon never made a
bigger blunder than when he allowed female
franchise to become law, for sooner or later
women’s influence, it is generally agreed, will give
the prohibition party absolute victory.

SATURDAY, MAY 12, 1894.
Maoriland’s Foreign Policy.
Every now and then Australasia is seized
with a violent yearning after a foreign policy,
and the privilege of taking a hand in the
disturbances of the outer world. Queensland
was afflicted with this complaint a few years
ago, when M‘ll wraith annexed New Guinea,
and conferred on the Papuan for a few brief
weeks the inestimable boon of being Queen
Victoria’s own cannibal. Julius Vogel
got it also during his last period of office,
when he conceived the great idea of annexing
unquiet Samoa, and holding that archipelago
down with the strong hand. Victoria, with
its perpetual desire to Calvinise the New
Hebrides, has the same complaint in a chronic
form. And now Maoriland has developed
it a second time, for the Seddon Cabinet has
revived Vogel’s project, and wants to take
over the Samoan difficulty, and the conflict-
ing interests of the various kings, and the
local wars and jealousies and national bank-
ruptcy, and all the rest of the many Samoan
troubles. Of all these attempts at creating
a foreign policy Seddon’s is certainly the
most foolish. M‘ll wraith’s New Guinea
project had at least some semblance of states-
manship about it, for New Guinea, if left to
its own fortunes, was certain to be annexed
sooner or later by some European power,
and it lies so close to Australia that its
annexation might bring an unpleasant
neighbour to our very gates. Therefore,
M Ilwraith’s scheme might be set down as
by no means a foolish precaution, and it
Involved no foreign complications in the
present but rather tended to prevent them in
the near future. Also, Vogel’s abortive pro-
ject had at least one negative merit, for at the
time that garish financier devised it the State
which annexed Samoa had only the native
difficulty to contend with, the awful complica-
tion of “ European interests” being still in a
comparatively undeveloped state. Moreover,
Vogel and MHlwraith both had in them
the element of the typical buccaneer, and,
given the opportunity, were capable of
annexing anything under the sun and holding
it tenaciously after it was annexed. They
had the instincts of the shark and the finer
qualities of the alligator, and were both
eminently fitted to extirpate the local
aborigine and otherwise pave the way for a
new civilisation.
» * *
The present annexation scheme of the
Seddon Cabinet is foolishness pure and sim-
ple. For years past Samoa has gone from
bad to worse, until it has become a hopeless
chaos. Missionary enterprise has spread a
thin veneer of canting piety over the local
savage, and every Samoan with the slightest
semblance to respectability has family wor-
ship daily, and keeps a big Scripture in a
conspicuous place in his domicile. The
hum of the niissionary hymn resounds
all through the archipelago, and so
does the din of the missionary church-
bell and the dolorous wail of the
church service in which the native convert
acknowledges with unction that he is a
miserable sinner. Meanwhile, a feeble,
half-hearted kind of civil war has become
almost chronic, and every now and then the
Sabbath calm is disturbed by that armed
skurry which in Samoa passes for a battle.
Then the pious church elder comes back,
dragging the head of his enemy in the
dust behind him, and the holy choir hauls the
mutilated dead along the streets. A strange,
unclean, scaly religion has taken possession
of the islands, and the saved aborigine of
to-day only differs from the lost aborigine of
a few years ago because he has added a
certain loathsome snuffle to his other sins.
Also, the islands are troubled by drink, and
leprosy, and Robert Louis Stevenson, with
his long hair and coat of velveteen, and
by the conflicting interests of Britain,
Germany, and the United States ; and the
alleged Government at Apia has no authority
whatever, and no prospect of acquiring any.
Therefore, whatever State annexes the group
must reckon on a general war with the whole
native population if it seriously proposes to
govern its new territory ; and it must needs
suppress Robert Louis Stevenson, and
steer a difficult course among the German
and American naval filibusters who have got
into the habit of turning their big guns on
the effete local Government; and it will
have to wipe out the foreign traders in arms
and ammunition, and to pick its devious
way among many conflicting sects and creeds;
and to sort out conflicting land-titles ; and
deal in many other shapes and forms with
the most complicated political problem in
the Pacific.
# # »
Maoriland is undoubtedly better fitted for
such an undertaking than any other Austra-
lasian province. It is the only one which
ever had any experience of actual war, and
in the old days of the Taranaki campaign, when
the local troops fought many hard battles at
places with unpronounceable names against
chiefs with nine-syllabled cognomens, it
gained just the sort of experience which is
wanted for a guerilla war in Samoa. But it
was a costly experience, and the bill for
these tattered glories of the early days
remains unpaid even till now. Maoriland,
more than any other Australasian province,
has reason to keep the peace and to abjure
all foreign politics. Premier Seddon’s great
idea is a tawdry theatrical device which
has nothing whatever to recommend it save
that it serves as a cheap advertisement for
himself. Even the most insatiable pro-
vincial filibuster can hardly regard Samoa as
worth having at the price. The native diffi-
culty could doubtless be surmounted, and
the missionary difficulty and the leprosy pro-
blem, and even the incubus of Robert Louis
Stevenson could be dealt with more or less
satisfactorily in course of time. But who-
ever takes over Samoa muse inherit along
with it more European jealousies and com-
plications than can be found in any other
patch of territory of the size, and the com-
munity which goes out of its way to hunt for
such troubles is just as imbecile as the indi-
vidual who buys an incurable complaint by
auction, or gets himself measured for a pain-
ful disease.
* w #
Premier Seddon’s project is apparently
one part Jingoism, one part cheap and shoddy
advertisement and promiscuous gaudiness,
and the balance made up of that hazy yearn-
ing about the “ Anglo-Saxon destiny ” with
which every tawdry title-seeking politician
in the empire, is more or less afflicted. In
consequence of this yearning, the empire
has already taken over some three
hundred millions of assorted niggers and
several hundred insoluble race problems, and
is fast getting to have more destiny on its
hands than it can manage. In this matter
Australasia has been more fortunate than the
most of those dominions on which the sun
never sets. It has only three native pro-
blems of its own on hand, and two of them,
at all events, are rapidly settling themselves.
The Maori is diminishing in numbers, and
whac remains of him can be assimilated with-
out injury. The black Australian is fast
dying out, being helped towards that desir-
able consummation by means which are
better not investigated. The Fijian native
is also fading away to some extent under
the blessed influences of civilisation, and
hopes are entertained in many well-
informed quarters that the Papuan, who
will make a fourth aboriginal problem to
be reckoned with in the near future,
will, with the benign assistance of the
Queenslann sugar - planter, do likewise,
and leave another blank among the
works of God. Still Australasia has quite
enough racial problems on hand, and
doesn’t want to annex any more. And,
above all things, it doesn’t want to acquire
the most chaotic archipelago in the South
Seas ; a group which is too small to be of
any serious value, and too far off to be
manageable, and where there is a separate
and individual disturbance to every square
mile of earth surface. The politician
who would annex Samoa under present
conditions is capable of annexing perdition.
Probably Seddon fancies that some prospec-
tive title, of a better kind than the common
or garden order of St. Michael and St.
George, is growing for him among the
Samoan reefs and palms, and this may
account for the fervour of his new-born
Jingoism. Failing this, a charitable genera-
tion may possibly ascribe his great idea to a
mere penny - dreadful and scalp - hunting
variety of enthusiasm, in order to avoid
some even less kindly explanation.
The Necessity of Suicide.
Editor Bulletin,— Whether Mr. Pecksniff
inculcates morality from Todgers’ banisters or
the Victorian Bench, his state must plainly be
diagnosed as clerical obfuscation. That the
judicial wisdom of the community should fail
to recognise the necessity of suicide says little
for judicial wisdom and not much for the com-
munity. Why, sir, suicide is the social safety-
valve. The stress of civilisation every day
forces a definite number of human beings to end
their own existence—so far as existence is cognis-
able by the law and the senses. The suicide
has no choice in the matter. The choice is that
of inexorable law, exacting a predetermined
sacrifice. The pressure of social forces is vented
along the line of least resistance : the weakest
go to the wall of life, and leap over into —
whatever there is on the other side.
• * *
Savage man has two prime needs—food and
females. Civilised man wants, in addition,
riches, ease, admiration, and many other things.
If the savage is weakly, he starves or is killed.
If the civilised man is weakly, he kills himself.
In either case, death is the token of defeat in
the battle of life. And, as it is to the interest
of society that the strong, not the weak, should
survive, suicide is a good and not an evil. Like
disease, it is. simply a natural means of
strengthening the breed by weeding out the
unfit. Were the weaklings permitted to live
and perpetuate their kind the whole race would
degenerate. In order that some at one end of
the social chain may find life worth living it is
absolutely necessary that some at the other end
be crushed out of existence. The strong thrive
by devouring the weak. Without suicides to
scold or criminals to hang and torture there
could be no Judge Madden, Don Quixote
was astonished to hear that the duchess owed
her fine health and splendid complexion, in the
first place to God, and in the next place to an
issue in her leg. Suicide is the issue in the leg
of society.
# * •
The alternative to suicide is crime. Where
suicide increases crime diminishes, and vice
versa. So far from the suicide failing in his
duty to society, it is really because his sense of
duty is strong that he is a suicide. When it is
weak, he is not a suicide, but a criminal.
Society is war. When an individual is worsted,
he may take his thrashing fighting or he may
take it lying down. The suicide takes it lying
down. The Anarchist takes it fighting.
Surely the man who only attacks himself is
preferable to the man who attacks others?
Perhaps, indeed, Judge Madden’s remarks
were due to the fact that his miserable target
had only “ attempted ” suicide. I must say I
hate people who half do things. But it
should be remembered in mitigation of
censure that the crude method of cutting one’s
throat may easily fail if not done with one
stroke. The letting of blood immediately
snaps the nervous tension which most people
must reach before they determine to “ end it.”
Then the suicide either faints or changes his
mind.
» • *
It is shown that the man who kills himself
commits no offence against society. But society
commits a serious offence against the man whom
it puts under the necessity of killing himself.
The lot of the great majority of mankind is to
pass through a life of misery to an eternity of
torture. The misery is patent everywhere ;
and to escape from it we must climb upon the
backs of those wallowing yet deeper in the
slough. As to the torture, only a “ little
flock ” can dodge the cheerful hell of
Christian dogma. Has not the individual
devoted to such a fate ground for complaint
against society ? Society unites a man and a
woman more or less unfit for parentage, and
he is born. Why ? Simply because he cannot
help himself. He did nob ask to be born if
he had any sense he did not want to be born ;
but society takes no account of his rights or
wishes. All he knows is that one fine morning,
noon, or night, he emerges to a consciousness
of entity ; and then his troubles begin. Pro-
bably he is born with inherited weakness,
malformation, or disease; but society does nob
care for that. If he is not lucky enough to be
one of the 30 per cent, of infants who die under
one year, or the 40-odd per cent, who die under
five years, he must linger through life as long
as he possibly can ; and, if he rebels, society
pays a red-faced man in a wig, and, perhaps, a
waxed moustache, large sums of money to call
him names and take away the little freedom
that is left him. Where is the justice of it ?
# « *
Suicide is physical insolvency. A man con-
fesses himself unable to meet the engagements
of life. Society helps the man who cannot
meet his commercial engagements to wipe them
out and start afresh. Why not similarly assist
the social failure to turn over a new leaf in
another world ? The least society can dp, after
having taken advantage of a man’s youth and
inexperience to shove him into existence, is to
help him out again when he is old and wise
enough to see the folly of living. Recognising
this, William Archer rationally suggests that
society should establish lethal chambers in every
centre of population, where those who are tired
of life might be painlessly and expeditiously
done to death, free of cost to themselves. Or
automatic machines might be placed at street-
corners to end the woes of existence, by the
simple process of putting a penny in the slot.
Doubtless some such ideas will be adopted when
humanity shall have advanced to a true com-
prehension of its ethical responsibilities.
# # *
In spite of Hamlet, there is no ground to
believe that the Everlasting has fixed His canon
’gainst self-slaughter. If He has, the suicide
doesn’t know it is loaded. Had the Everlasting
any objection to suicides, it is the easiest thing
in the world to prevent them. A turn of the
wrist of Omnipotence, and we might be per-
fectly happy instead of profqundly wretched.
But Omnipotence isn’t built that way. It has
always seemed to me a curious thing that one
Everlasting should spend time and pains in
making a charming universe, then allow another
Everlasting to come in and play up old Harry
with creation, and put the blame on creation.
Just as if a great artist were to paint a splendid
picture and go and hire an unemployed to chuck
mud at it, afterwards telling the picture—“ It’s
all your fault for getting dirty !” Guy de
Maupassant thinks that the present mode of
race-perpetuation is very crude. It lacks deli-
cacy and refinement. He could have arranged
things much better. So could I.— Brigham
Brown, M.D., F.R.O.S.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Pastor Marshall and Deacon Skinner.
Rev. Alexander Marshall, of Melbourne, is
kind enough to mention from the proud eleva-
tion of his preaching perch that theatre-goers
are nob necessarily vicious. He himself has
been twice to theatres—once when Walter
Bentley got hold of the clerical lamb by its
wool and dragged it along to see “The Silver
King,” and for a second time quite recently,
when he did, with the pious intention of finding
worldly material for a sermon, purchase six-
pennyworth of gallery contemplation of ‘ ‘ Struck
Oil.” Situated away up amongst the unre-
generate near the roof, Rev. Marshall gazed
through the shimmer of gas heated atmosphere
upon a phase of the drama which displeased
him very much. In the character of Deacon
Skinner this godly “ god ” saw “ religion cari-
catured and held up to odium,” and he cannot
altogether approve of a form of entertainment
that includes items such as John Stofel’s
mortgagee. He will not consign theatre-
goers to damnation merely on account of Deacon
Skinner, he will not even assert that the
Church is free from snuffling hypocrisy ; but he
thinks it “ unfair to Christianity and unjust to
religion that such a hateful exception should be
represented as a normal fact.” Mr. Marshall
talked much more in the same strain, and
always on the false assumption that Clnistianity
and religion are inseparable from regular
Church attendance and parsonic influence. In-
asmuch as the fictitious villain, Skinner, was a
black-coated deacon, the rev. critic considered
him an unfair reflection on the doctrines of
Christ, a distinct wrong to the cause of modern
righteousness. Skinner, in sober truth, belongs
to one of the most useful ami legitimate types
of stage character. There is a moral in him,
the moral that professional piety creates profes-
sional humbug by encouraging the vulgar error
that respectability is allied to psalm-singing
and folding of the hands in prayer. If the
Church did not, by its lamentable pulpit
accents and tea-meeting manners, lay down a
code of outward signs of inward holiness there
would be no room for Deacon Skinner in
melodrama. He has no reference to real
Christianity, but is just a plain, coarse reminder
that church Christianity wears a badge easily
imitated by any despicable frauds who wish to
trade upon it. He holds those despicable
frauds up to odium, and if the genuine
article suffers through its resemblance to
the caricature, the genuine article has only
itself to blame. Now, as regards Mr. Mar-
shall’s contention that hypocrisy is a morbid
“ excrescence” (Mr. Marshall is Presbyterian
by persuasion) of Melbourne religion and “not
a normal growth.” Gan the good man consider
the story of the land-boom and its incidental
frauds, and conscientiously deny that the
tabernacles absolutely stink of Deacon Skinner-
ism? The smug, fraudulent saint is counted.by
hundreds, and thousands in Smellbourne. He
is the worm that has eaten away the hearts
of the banks, and consumed the substance
of men and women who trusted- in his
precious character. He gave freely to ■ the
church that he might victimise the congrega-
tions. He formed a long, wide, and • deep
unavowed conspiracy for deluding the .public
with a glamour of personal propriety. He was
a mighty power in Parliament, and-still crows
a mild spiritual crow upon the debris of his
former immaculateness. He even accomplished
an act of poetic justice by swallowing up the
savings of many parsons in his sanctimonious
gamble. Deacon Skinner not a normal fact—
The Bulletin smiles !
Bruiser Madden, C.J., and the Cat.
Chief Justice Madden, of Victoria, who, the
other day, talked such foolishness to a
would-be suicide, has been “orating” at
large again—this time on the question of
wife-desertion. Judge Madden’s remedy for
wife-desertion is the cat; a breezy and
vigorous flogging, in his opinion, is the best
thing to promote peace in disorganised families,
and to create harmony and good feeling
generally. The case under discussion was one
in which the desertion followed almost imme-
diately after the marriage, the husband having
apparently realised almost as soon as the
wedding was over that it was a failure.
The judicial assumption was that he had
entered upon his solemn undertaking in a
frivolous spirit, as a mere experiment or passing
amusement; and Judge Madden considered
that a vigorous flogging was the only thing
which would waken such as he to a sense of
their responsibilities. “ There were,” he said,
indignantly, “designing vagabonds who made
use of the form of marriage as a mere
cloak for the baser desires of human


nature,” and he was convinced that
“authority should be given, in such cases, to
order flogging, as he could see no other means
of checking an evil that had grown to terrible
dimensions.” Judge Madden, as a patron of
bruisers and a man who has, .from time
to time, derived amusement from seeing
one hulking individual bash another out
of shape, has probably become morally
deteriorated, and his moral deterioration may
account for his ferocity, though not for his
hideous logic. Therefore, The Bulletin wishes
to ask him several questions. It wants to know
(1) how many marriages Mr. Madden supposes
are built absolutely on a spiritual or intel-
lectual plan, and how many on the “ baser de-
sires of human nature ” ? Does Mr Madden feel
like taking an affidavit that there is 5 per cent,
of the spiritual element —or even2J? (2) When
Mr. Madden complains about the baser desires
of human nature being cloaked by marriage
does it mean that he would prefer these desires
—which exist and can’t be got rid of —in their
undressed state and hot cloaked by anything?
(3) What is Mr. Madden’s opinion about the
future happiness of the reunited family where
the husband has been brought to a deeper sense
of his responsibilities, and awakened to a
knowledge of his duties as a husband, by means
of the cat in the hands of the common hang-
man ? (4) What is Mr. Madden’s view of the
feelings of a wife whose lost husband is re-
turned to her under such conditions ? (5)
Would Mr. Madden propose to flog
wives who desert their husbands for no
apparent cause except the baser desires of
human nature, as well as husbands who desert
their wives? Wives who run away are
certainly few, and husbands are many, but the
reason of that is, mainly, that the wife who
leaves her husband generally goes away to
starve, while the husband who leaves his wife
gets rid of a burden and a responsibility. In
any case, the offence, when it is committed, is
the same ; therefore, if Mr. Madden doesn’t
propose to flog the absconding wife, why doesn’t
he ? Further, it often happens that a wife
would gladly desert her husband, but as, by
doing so, she would lose her sole means of sup-
port, she refrains, and, instead, is unfaithful to
him at home, and, as her sin is practically the
same as desertion, would Mr. Madden sug-
gest that she should also be flogged? Like-
wise, would he flog a man under similar
conditions, and, if not, then why not? (6) Again,
if flogging is beneficial to arouse a man, under
the above conditions, to a sense of his responsi-
bilities, how would it come in to awake him to
a sense of his responsibilities at other crises of
his life —say when he is going to take shares in
a dubious company, or to stand for Parliament?
Furthermore, The Bulletin would point out
to its learned brother, Madden, that there is
no evidence whatever that wife-desertion is
increasing—in fact, there is every probability
that it is diminishing. Shiels’ Act, however,
enables the deserted wife to make known her
wrongs in the Divorce Court instead of endur-
ing them in silence, and Mr. Madden, being a
shallow person with no true sense of his responsi-
bilities, and who uses his billet as a cloak for
the base desire of his human nature to be re-
ported in the papers, jumps to a wrong con-
clusion accordingly. And the fact that Judge
Madden can “conceive - no other means of
checking the evil” in question save by flogging
only proves that he is as bald in the conception
as in the head; it doesn’t prove by any
means that flogging would check the evil.
IE he were a patron of Buffalo Bill shows
instead of slogging-matches he would pro-
bably be unable to conceive any other remedy
than scalping, but that also would prove noth-
ing. Up to date the law has made no serious
attempt to try what imprisonment, penal
servitude, hard labour, solitary confinement or
sticking matches under people’s nails, to say no-
thing of tooth-pulling and nose-clipping, will do
in such cases, and until it has exhausted them the
Judge who asserts that there is no remedyfor
wife-desertion save flogging merely makes a
gratuitous ass of himself and talks through his
hat,

Wise’s “ Liberal Party.”
Parkes’ gushful follower, B. R. Wise, spoke a
piece recently at a Foreign-trade meeting at
Redfern (Sydney), and took the opportunity to
introduce a new term into N.S.W. politics. In
the course of his address he alluded at least 18
times to the “Liberal Party.” If Wise and
Co. are “ Liberals,” it, of course, follows that
everybody else is Conservative. These terms
won’t acclimatise in Australia. They are merely
the signs of Mr. Wise’s English university train-
ing, and his yearning to be English in all his
manners and expressions, and his blastiferous
contempt for whatever isn’t English but Aus-
tralian. Australia has got a Democratic Party,
and it has gone far beyond the “ Liberal” idea.
That English word has about it an odour of
pauperism. It suggests the patronising attitude
of the vicar and the squire, who are prepared
to make liberal concessions to the—haw —lower
ordahs, provided they , are duly submissive and
respectful, and pull their aggregate forelock to
their“betters.” Itsuggestsalms, benevolenceand
the lofty condescension of the superiah being. A
man isn’t spoken of as being liberal to himself ,;
he is liberal to other people. Therefore, the
Liberal Party is the party which is prepared to
give something as a favour to the masses;
while the Conservative Party is the party which
isn’t prepared to give anything. The Demo-
cratic Party, on the other hand, is the one
which is prepared, not to give a certain measure
of political and social equality as a favour, but
to take it in its entirety as a matter of right.
The Liberal is the representative of dignified
and lofty patronage ; the Democrat, of absolute
justice. “ Liberalism ” suggests Samuel Mor-
ley ; Democracy, George Washington. There-
fore, Australia doesn’t want any “ Liberal ”
Party, and throws the epithet back with
derision.

Grey on tne “Anglo-Saxon Destiny.”
Even , pkl George Grey seems to have been
seized with the old Anglo-Saxon complaint
almost as soon as he touched British soil. The
following scream, transmitted by cable, is a
depressing thing to come from such a source :
In an interview with a representative of the press. Sir
George Grev dwelt upon the destiny of the Anylo-Saxon
race, the federal ion of which, he said, would dominate
the world, with its council meeting at London, Washing-
ton, Sydney, Melbourne and Auckland.
Truly the saddest thing upon this earth is that
blessed “ Anglo-Saxon destiny.” Nobody
knows exactly what it is, or where it, is, or
whether there is really any such thing
at all, or whether, the Anglo-Saxon—if there
is any Anglo-Saxon—hasn’t already had all
the destiny he is ever going to have. But it
seems to be generally assumed that some-
where ahead in the dim future there is to be
a hazy and explosive culmination, or apex, or
pinnacle, or moral elevation, or something,
and the man who thinks he is an Anglo-Saxon
looks forward to this in much the same way as
he looks forward to the millennium—if there is
any millennium. Meanwhile, Grey makes a
pitiable mistake by going into particulars about
this shadow. The United States isn’t Anglo-
Saxon, in any case—even supposing there is any
Anglo-Saxon at all—any more than it is Celtic
or African. If the federation did come
about it wouldn’t dominate the world. Also,
its council wouldn’t “meet at London, Wash-
ington, Sydney, Melbourne, and Auckland,”
because Ottawa and Vancouver and Cape Town
and Dublin and San Francisco and various
other places would see it hanged first before
they would be left out, and there would be such
a row in Hobart as Grey doesn’t even dream of.
And, in any case, the alleged Anglo-Saxon
doesn’t so much want to get any more destiny
as to wash the destiny he has got already.
Even General Booth, pottering feebly around
in his efforts to do something in the immediate
present for the “ submerged tenth,” is of more
use to humanity than the patriot who yearns
over the future destiny of the race unto seventy
times seven.
That blessed fiction, the poor old cableman’s
“ bomb,” is again to the fore. In the present
instance, the dismal tale started under the
heading, in huge black letters, “ Another Bomb
Discovered. Narrow Escape of Royalty ; ” and
then it went on to tell how some miscreant
planted the awful explosive in the Guildhall,
and how the Duchess of Connaught and her
children were within a few yards of it, and
how, but for the valour of somebody who ex-
tinguished the fuse, they would have been
blown to rags. Everybody was so dread-
fully shocked for some time after that
hardly anyone noticed that the gruesome
bomb story was falling to pieces all the
time. First, the cableman acknowledged
that the Duchess wasn’t really “ within a few
yards ; ” she was only in an adjacent gallery.
Next the bomb-fuse w;asn’t alight; next the bomb
wasn’t loaded; and finally it transpired that the
thing was no bomb after all. In short, there
was nothing whatsoever in the yarn, but all the
same the vague impression remains that royalty
had a narrow escape, and that the loathsome
anarchist needs to be put down with tremen-
dous energy. During the same week several
more “ dynamite outrages ” were reported from
different parts of Europe, and in most of
them the bomb was providentially discovered
by the police when the fuse wasn’t alight.
Then it was naturally taken to police head-
quarters, and the valorous force presently
issued its own unsupported statement about
the deadly nature of the contents. For plant-
ing a harmless meat-tin where it can be re-
captured later on under sensational conditions,
and then reporting at great length on the awful
nature of the contents, the police and their
prophet, the cableman, have no equals.
An English Tory paper is of opinion that
anarchism will never be “ stamped out ” till
the ordinary processes are suspended and sus-
pected anarchists are tried by court-martial
and executed off-hand. The Bulletin cor-
dially endorses this view. Neither anarchism
nor anything else will be stamped out till
martial law—-which is simply a process whereby
the innocent and the guilty are hanged or shot
together without ceremony —is substituted for
the slow, uncertain, and troublesome methods
of the civil code. And neither anarchism
nor anything else will be stamped out
even then. No crime ever is stamped out,
in fact, by any sudden upheaval of law
whatever. Certain forms of crime gradually
diminish, no doubt, in the slow procession of
the ages, simply because the world grows tired
of practising them, or because it finds them
useless and unprofitable and uninteresting ; but
the sudden “stamping-out” theory is pure
idiocy. The law has been hanging men with
ghastly regularity for many centuries to stamp
out murder, and it isn’t stamped out yet. It is
only kept within certain limits, but within
these limits it doesn’t fluctuate to any great
extent. Now and then the law puts on an extra
spurt of hanging with a view to putting
down the crime for good and all, and then
it generally increases rather than diminishes.
The essence of the Hospital Saturday move-
ment is simply the use of sex-attraction to
“raise the wind,” The employment of young
women to blandish pennies out of recalcitrant
pockets is quite as objectionable as the similar
employment of young women as barmaids— if
that be objectionable. Hear the collectors’
experiences, as detailed in the daily press.
One says :
We have had nothing but kindness from everyone.
There are two charming young girls with me, and,
perhaps, that has a sort of—you know what I mean.
Another:
One old mm, who said he had already given, and then
passed by,- met one of the young ladies a little way down
the street. She is a very pretty Jewess. He stopped and
gave her half a sovereign, telling her it was just because
she was-a Jewess.
He said, this nice old man, that “he liked to see
things done without regard to sect or religion.”
“ But,” added the collector, parenthetically,
“she’s a very pretty girl.” Exactly. And one of
the collectors complained that the women ap-
pealed to seldom gave her a cent. Why should
they? But organise another Hospital Satur-
day, with well-dressed, dapper, waxed-mous-
tachioed young men as collectors, and the women
will part like lambs —if their husbands and
brothers find the funds. The Bulletin would
back a just-imported English dude, with check
pants, tan shoes, a drawl, and a gold-headed
cane to suck, against the prettiest girl in
Sydney—one collecting from the women and
the other from the men. How much would
men give to male beggars ?

One of Premier Patterson’s funny little “re-
trenchment ” jokes is a Parliamentary com-
mittee to report on suitable sites for a new
Melbourne cemetery. Sundry syndicates are
seeking to trade off their land for boneyard
purposes, and a number of patriots are travel-
ling around, or sitting in town at so much per
diem, to discover a convenient and wholesome
resting-place for the dead. With this end in
view they summon undertakers and grave-stone
masons, who naturally discuss the alluring
subject of Christian burial from an un-
prejudiced standpoint. For instance, no under-
dertaker considers that the soil is good for cold
remains when it happens to be so far off that a
majestic, unbroken funeral is out of the
question. He believes in giving the corpse’s
friends a fair chance to “hearse it” all the
way, instead of being obliged to pop the coffin
into a cemetery-train which runs right up to
the ground, and leaves no room for elaborate
obsequies. Monumental dealers also object to a
remote God’s acre for the reason that it tempts
loving husbands to erect small marble crosses to
Maria’s memory, whereas they would order
huge masses of granite if the cemetery were a
handy, fashionable resort, and the tombstones
were more exposed to public criticism. But
the point on which both branches of the trade
wax eloquent together is cremation. They
despise the bare suggestion of burning bodies
for 12s. fid. and putting the ashes into an urn
for the parlour mantel-shelf. Each undertaker
objects to cremation on “ sentimental grounds,”
whilst every mason shakes his head silently at
the committee and makes a few hazy remarks
about the Christian religion. So in due course
Parliament will .doubtless receive a report
stating that such and such a soil is best calcu-
lated to give pleasure to interments, and that
the feeling of the country is even more strongly
in favour of worms than before.

The second edition of the Speight v. Syme trial
is, if that be possible, several times drearier
and more abjectly prosy than the first. There
is the same evidence dragged out of the same
witnesses by the same tedious process of ex-
amination and re-examination and cross-
examination. The same counsel make much
the same woeful jokes as they did before.
The shorthand-writers take down the same old
testimony once more ; the newspapers tell the
dismal tale all over again ; the court yawns
drearily and shows the same gold-stopping in
its back-tooth as aforetime. All the interest—
if there ever was any interest —has gone out of
the ancient repetition. And, considering that
the whole thing can be found in its entirety in
the notes of the last trial and the reports of the
speeches and the summing-up, it would seem to
any unbiassed lay person that the new
jurymen might just as well have these
documents read over to them —any unemployed
actor with a good voice and a reasonable
knowledge of elocution could be hired for the
job—and give their'verdict accordingly. They
would get all the facts that way, and the only
thing they would lose would be the irrelevant
witticisms of Purves and Duffy and Deakin,
which have nothing at all to do with the case.
Also, they would get through the business in
one-fourth the time ; fully 95 per cent, of the
costs would be saved ; and the Judge might go
away and employ himself usefully by hanging
somebody in the time which he now wastes
over a twice-told tale. If the Law is capable
of taking in a new idea—which is more than
doubtful—it might seriously consider this one.
As far as can be gathered, the shearers do not
view the prospect of a strike with any
enthusiasm. Leaders and organisers talk big ;
but the rank-and-file are silent, though,
like the sailor’s parrot, they think a lot.
Officially, a strike is announced; privately,
the shearers are filling the sheds. The
shearers are wise. This is no time for a
strike, when the country is full of unemployed
labour, when every bush holds a beggar for a
billet, and for every man who throws down his
shears there are a score ready to pick them
up. The pastoralists know this, they have
ample funds, their time is well chosen, and they
have deliberate!}' ignored the unions in altering
the agreement, reducing the wages, and making
conditions of labour more stringent. A strike !
they wish for nothing better ; for they foresee
the inevitable collapse, and they hope that,
this time, collapse means complete ruin. Why
play into their hands ? The funds to be spent
on a strike would be much better spent in
settling the shearers on the lands of the
N.S.W. central division. And a strike is a
poor argument, anyhow. The ballot-box is
an irresistible one. The pastoralists dictate
because they have the political power. Let
labour get due representation and the
pastoralists will gladly come to terms. And,
if every man be true to himself and the cause
of Democracy, the coming N.S.W. election
should do more for the shearers than many,
many strikes.
Two enemies of labour in politics—the
Fat Man and the Fad Man.
Henry Wrixon’s proposal to resign from
Yic. Assembly, ere departing for Canada, has
been received by the press with a moan of despair.
Victoria, it seems, can ill afford to lose the
services of great minds like Wrixon’s. He is
necessary to the “restoration of confidence,” the
increase of natural products, the respectability of
public life, and so on. But no instances of
Wrixon’s usefulness are discoverable, all the same.
Beyond his Pharisaical bill to prohibit Sunday
newspapers, Wrixon has accomplished nothing
that The Bulletin can call to mind. He has sat
in a malodorous Legislature without making any
public effort to clear the atmosphere—nay, more,
he has always joined in the “Sssh !” chorus when
any coarse person proceeded to make a vulgar row
with a view of drawing attention to the bad smell.
As a leisured barrister and expert draftsman, he
might have introduced an honest measure for the
prevention of barefaced roguery in limited com-
panies’ liquidation, but he didn’t. Wrixon has
been merely a rich lawyer sitting silent and
eminently “respectable” amidst evidence of dis-
honest law-making and law-faking. For the sake
of democracy it may be hoped that he will stop
aw:ay from Australia.

The two “armies” of unemployed who
were said to be moving on Washington with a view
to general devastation seem to be mostly a cable-
graphic invention. The first army, which started
from California, was to consist of 25,000 men, but
it only gathered 19 deadbeats all told. By the
time it reached Texas it had swelled to fifty men,
and a month ago this force was stranded at the
lonesome town of El Paso, on the Mexican
border. The second army, led by the much-
advertised Coxey, was to muster 50,000 men, but
only 75 turned up, and of these 52 gave up the
contract the very first day. The expedition was
then plodding miserably through Ohio amid
heavy snowstorms, and appeared to have no definite
purpose whatever. Its leader is a rich man who
is vaguely described as a “ free silver theosophist
he also believes that he contains part of the soul
of Jesus Christ, and that the balance of it is mostly
embodied in his partner, Carl Browne. Both of
them are badly deranged on the subject of Revela-
tions. Coxey and his “officers” travelled in a
waggon and put up at hotels, and the “army”
walked and foraged for itself. The commander
of this circus seems to be, to all intents and pur-
poses, a harmless lunatic with spectacles and a
far away expression; and his following is ap-
parently animated mainly by humorous motives
and a desire to take a hand in anything that may
turn up.

My Dear Moorabinda—
The official opening of the Women’s College
within Sydney University, on the sth of merry
May, might have been an ordinary reception with
ice-cream trimmings, for to all outward seeming it
was a crush of well-dressed people—principally
women (though one daughter of Eve with full
sail on fills the eye more than three men) all on
tho nearest approach to a broad grin that society
permits itself. As if, in point of fact, the Women’s
College were more of a rich joke in ’Varsity circles
than an educational white elephant that ought to
be taken as seriously—as it will be when the bills
come in.
I was stupid enough to miss the inaugural pro-
ceedings by Lady Duff (squired by Mr. Gallon with
a pearl scarf-pin on), and arrived in time to see the
company crowding into the tea-room as if to sup-
port the allegation that brain-workers require
more refreshments than hod-carriers. Despite its
eagerness to reach the sandwiches, the crowd was
most picturesque. If I were a ’Varsity girl 1
would certainly pursue those branches of book-
learning that lead to a tomato-red hood turned
up with rabbit-skin—it looks so striking on fete
days. I think the most conspicuous object was
the educational Pooh-Bah in a bright red gown,
like the Cardinal or Portia, and the oddest turn-
out among a muster of really stylish dresses had
leather straps and buckles where buttons usually
occur. Dow a woman with that taste in trimming
must envy a horse its harness!
The first Women’s College in Australia is a very
large red building with its face turned to suit the
road and not the weather. In this summery land
no. well-regulated fagade should face the west, and
Die broad front balcony and verandah of this
Temple of Minerva will be hot enough to roast the
girl-graduates whole in their jackets in seven
mouths’ time. It was a full-dress rehearsal at the
College on Saturday, I may tell you. In one
room (there are 26 bedrooms) you saw the snowy
white bed, the erudite well-thumbed books (the
novels were all up the chimney for the time being),
the afternoon tea-service and the whole setting of
the studentesses, who are very cheerfully housed, if
they aren’t depressed by the fact that the present
number of feminine seekers after higher-grade
knowledge fills the big building about as cosily as
three split peas fill a 400-gallon tank. In the
dining-room the point of admiration is a beautiful
freeze round the top of the wall. I haven’t spelt
it correctly—there oughtn’t to be two e’s—but,
then, one’s thoughts run on frosts somehow at the
W.’s College.
There is one reassuring thing to be said. Now
that Potts Point has got its pet “intellectual”
fad done into solid mortgageable bricks and
mortar, it has got its whim for better or worse,
and whenever society thirsts for an opportunity to
perform in public, the Women’s College—doomed
to be the horse-leech’s daughter among public
institutions—will be an evergreen excuse for Miss
deVere to show off her skirt-dance at five shil-
lings per head. Whether society will get “Jack”
of providing hay for its white elephant is a horse
of another colour.
There are 12 men to balance four women in the
Bauble Shop at Sydney Lyceum, so, of course,
I liked it—even better than “Mrs. Tanqueray,”
which was scarcely a ladies’ play, for it seemed
to slap women in the face so. There is one tip-
top dress in the “ Bauble Shop,” worn by Mrs.
Brough—cream satin with big sleeves, and waist-
tags of green velvet, white lace epaulettes and
diamonds; and as Mrs. Brough is forced to do
the scene—like a peacock tied to a dingy balus-
trade—merely to lighten the black-coated aspect
of the 3rd act, it vvas just as well she was rigged
so handsomely. Emma Temple, attired in pale-
grey silk with a saucy blue velvet jacket edged
with brown fur and a hat to match, probably
decided the winter fashions for half the women in
the stalls, and Miss Noble, as the supernaturally
“green” ingenue, wore brown—the livery of
stage virtue in the humbler walks of life—also of
the laughing-jackass.
By the way, Boucioault’s brown coat only
wanted half a mile or so of “serpentine” braid
and a dart at the waist to look like the fashionable
feminine jacket of the moment, but otherwise the
tailoring left nothing to cavil at. The new man,
Mr. Franklyne, turned out to be an eagle-hawk
youth who paints his low-comedy mouth a vivid
scarlet, and who has learnt the value of catching
the eye of the gallery, judging by the frequency
with which his gaze travelled Olympia-wards.
It is unjust to fudge him from one ap-
pearance, but, so far, he has exhibited no
overwhelmingly satisfactory reason why he
should have invaded this country, except to
make the movement afore-mentioned, and give
the dress - circle a chance to fritter away
another bit of its fresh young affections. Which
reminds me that a Sydney girl had a birthday the
other day, and when her kind sister asked her
what she would like for a gift the damsel replied,
“ Give me Titheradge’s portrait.” The wrongs of
a married man do not make him sacred to im-
pressionable maidenhood when he happens to be
an actor.
There are generally two sides to every question
—but it is really pleasant to discover that His
maladroit Excellency of Titipu has a contra-
acoount of bad manners against the Titiputiaus to
balance his own tidy soore. Or, at least, his wife
has. Only the other day a muster of women
approached Lady Blank and begged her to fix a
date for meeting at Government House to arrange
the Whens and Hows of a big charity ball, also
to adorn the proceedings as president. And Lady
Blank graciously (when a vice-regal personage is
only ordinarily amiable the “ very best people ”
always employ that adjective) fixed the one and
consented to the other.
When the day arrived, one or two ladies who
keep an eagle-eye on their engagement-tablets
turned up to time at the vice-regal portal, and
found Lady Blank arranged in the drawing-room
waiting for the crowd to accumulate. And she
might be waiting still as far as that conscienceless
she-committee was concerned, for none of them
toed the scratch or sent in a written apology, and,
after giving the whole herd ten minutes’ grace.
Lady Blank gracefully withdrew from what should
have been the scene of action, conscious that her
side scored heavily in that round, at all events.
And yet these embryo committee-ladies (who
are, doubtless, still by the ears over the blunder—
a woman always blames her pals, and not herself,
if anything gangs agley) are not altogether in the
wrong. I think some of the contumely ought to
fall on the vice-regal p.s., whose mechanical duty
it is to advertise all such symposia in the dailies,
but who, in this case, hadn’t even a hint of it
either in the papers or in his book of official dates.
After all, it’s to be hoped that the ball won’t
fizzle out like a damp squib because a parcel of
short-mernoried women are in the habit of seeking
in the newspapers for their daily engagements.
Dr. and Miss M'Donagh were “at home ” to a
number of guests last Thursday afternoon, ehiefest
of the throng being one or two Austrian officers
off the Fasana (who are popular in Sydney because
of their English-speaking qualities), in whose
honour the “at home ” was held. Miss M'Donagh,
in lavender silk with a bodice of maroon velvet,
made a cordial hostess, and vocal and pianoforte
items supported the buzz of talk—M. Kowalski
playing the beautiful Austrian hymn in com-
pliment to the officers. Dainty refreshments
were served in the dining room during the after-
noon to the guests, amongst whom were Lady
Windeyer, Mrs. Eddy, in a stylish grey gown;
Mrs. Arthur Bennett, Mrs. Hubert Kelly, Mrs.
Backhouse and many more.
r i he season of charity balls is upon us, and the
working and Factory Girls’ Club ball promises to
open it so brilliantly, on the 16th inst. in Sydney
.Town Hall, that other charity-ball committees
will have hard work to keep up the record,
i here are to be fancy sets of all sorts and a sailor’s
hornpipe to keep the gallery spectators amused,
and late trams and trains make getting home the
easiest thing in the world. Tickets are eight
shillings each or three for one guinea, and Mrs.
Douglas Mackenzie, Mr. Justice Innes and Mr.
W. H. Edgar are honorary secretaries to what
promises to be the best public ball of the coming
season.
The snow-ball of Governor Duff’s unpopularity
grows apace, and now he has capped his Highland-
gathering exploit by snubbing the Guv’ment in
the recent Thetis affair. To put it plainly, the
Premier and the Colonial Treasurer invited Sir
Robert Duff to go to Grafton on the Government
steamer; but when His Ex. inspected the craft
that has so often carried Toby Barton and his
fortunes up the Hawkesbury, he found it to be
“too small,” and coolly informed the Treasurer,
one of his hosts, that he, Mr. See, would have to
travel by some o-her boat.
That was the signal for the scalping to com-
mence. “ Love me, love my Ministers” is Premier
Dibbs’ sentiment, and the questionable taste of
the Governor in taking it upon himself to weed out
his entertainers aroused the long politician to
“open wrath.” When he met His Excellency
there was back talk. The tall personage gave the
small potentate a bit of his mind without its
Windsor uniform on, and the result is another
avenue closed by which His Excellency might
have won his way to the loving ’art of the nation.
On dit that a strongly-worded petition, with a
regular _ comet’s tail of signatures, will go to
“ town ” before long; and meanwhile it is amus-
ing to hear some of the newspapers piping their
indignation against the Governor for not “invit-
ing ” the Premier on the red-letter voyage referred
to. As a matter of fact, Premier Dibbs refused
to join the party when Gov. Duff started to
govern it with the forte pedal down, so to speak,
Madame Lucien Henry’s thoughtful lecture on
the peasant-painter, Millet, at Sydney Y.M.0.A.,
on Monday evening, attracted a “ society ” house.
The Duff girls, with their governesses, were
among the audience, who received Madame Henry
(handsomely dressed in black satin with amber
satin panels) in a very cordial spirit, and with
many floral offerings. Madame Henry lectures in
a calinly-conversational style, very easy to follow,
and although the Y.M.C.A. isn’t the quietest
place in Sydney to harangue in, I think everybody
could hear, although Madame dispensed with a
sounding-board, and the Y.M.C.A. youths seemed
to be holding a bicycle-meeting on the floor above,
interspersed with shower-baths.
Madame Henry, whoso portrait appears else-
where in The Bulletin, lectures again on the
28th. She is a gracious woman, and by far the
most impressive daughter of France whom we
have had with us. To give the story of Madame’s
life, which began, as so many French comedies
begin, with a convent-training and a marriage by
parental order, would be to anticipate a story
which none can relate so graphically as the
heroine. Melodrama itself seems tame beside the
tale of this woman, who has experienced all the
vicissitudes of fortune, the extremes of wealth and
poverty, the dangers of the battlefield, exile, and
hunger in a foreign land, where sheer hard work
and pluck conquered an honourable position for
herself and her children.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, May 7, 1894.
Shakespeare’s birthday was made the occasion
for a soulful afternoon-tea at the Austral Salon.
A choice blend of sassiety and literary “lights”
attended “oulchaw’s ’’rendezvous to devour tattle
and cake, with homoeopathic doses of the bard
thrown in. The wild-eyed daughters of the Pen
(large P for Pen) were present in great force, and
to diversify a flighty display of new
season’s fashions by their severely masculine style
of dress.
A group of rather pretty girls, with toques
nestling daintily on their artistically-coiled tresses,
formed a striking contrast to their companions,
who at the first glance gave one the impression
of a stage-stricken younger brother or Byronio
cousin. Closer scrutiny proved this apparition to
be a local editress, wearing a vest, tie, tweed
jacket, and cropped head.
Another of the guild was conspicuous in a
crimson cravat, so that, to an unemanoipated eye,
Mdlle. Marie Liet, helping to hand round the
Salong souchong, and looking sweetly nice the
while, came as a blessed relief.
By the way, I hear this same young lady is
having her picture painted by a local artist, one
who is able to entertain his charming sitter and
her ma by fluent converse in their native tongue
whilst he works the colour in.
Lady Hopetoun, in her present r6le of a pilgrim
of sisterly love, has been undergoing dangers by
flood and field. (See Queensland papers). The
rivers are running bankers, and the
Bishop of those parts is shut up in his diocese and
couldn’t get to Melb. for last Tuesday’s ecclesiasti-
cal junketing, so the former part of the descrip-
tion may not be overdrawn. But, if there were
any “perils by field’’ to encounter, it was pro-
bably the Cairns people who suffered rather than
her sporting Ladyship, as shown by the following
narrative :
Some time back in a country district hereabouts
the Countess set off one morning on a hare-shoot-
ing expedition, driving a dog-cart, which looked a
tight fit for herself and her hostesses (two lively
sisters-in-law.) They soon returned, crestfallen
and out of temper, and complained that hardly
had they started a bunny and begun blazing away
than from the opposite bank of the small river,
which babbled at their feet, arose a cry as of one
in mortal extremity. Hasty search disclosed a
crouching in the bracken, who declared he
was ‘ nearly shot,” though there were no traces of
plebeian gore to account for his prostrate condi-
tion and moans. After orolonged discussion and
much clinking of coin, he confessed that he had
sustained no injury, but that an alarming
explosion of firearms, accompanied by a whistling
of shot through his whiskers, had given him a
fright he wouldn’t forget till Judgment Day.
I bis was consoling, but the incident had spoilt
the outing and the ladies retired from the scene in
a chastened frame of mind.
By-the-way, the Countess, en route to Cairns,
annexed the lively daughter of a Northern
Governor. Perhaps she thinks that the best way
to cure an attack of calf-love is to provide the
sufferer with a new and attractive peg to hang his
affections on.
Grass widowerhood is all the rage at present, and
though “the first gentleman of the land ” didn’t
enjoy a former experience, as testified by his un-
shorn chin and general air of sackcloth and ashes,
he seems fairly reconciled to divided bliss nowa-
days. Those who know say it is a habit which
grows on one, and an easily-acquired taste. This
adventurous better-half started for Bananaland in
excellent spirits—spirits that a wine-merchant-
would describe as overproof. Before, when from
a stern sense of duty, she tore herself away from
home and hubby, she boarded the P. and 0., with
beetroot nose and eyes, and not even a veil drawn
over her tear-stained cheeks. This dejection wasn’t
evanescent either, for she was never seen to smile
until the last week of her journey, when one of
the ship s officers flashed upon her with a witti-
cism, which it had taken him the whole trip to
perfect. Thence the frozen fountains of her mirth
broke up andshe laughed until the alarmed joker,
like the man in the poem, made a vow that never
again would he be as funny as he could.
Preachers are beginning to find texts, not only
m stones and running brooks, as recommended by
the poet, but in theatres, racecourses, and similar
lively spots. The pastor of Scots Church has
been dealing largely in novelties lately, with the
result that outsiders crowd out the rightful pew-
owners, several of whom the other evening
crouched on the cold floor all through the service,
and looked injured.
Go-ahead as Mr. Marshall is, however, he’s not
quite so much so as some people give him credit
a' t hear ,? a Paragraph, something to this
effect, appeared in a Scotsman, dated about two
years back. “The minister of ‘Soots Church,’
Australia, finding that his congregations have
been falling off, has hit upon a new device for
filling the kirk. He has ordered that all his choir
shall wear kilts. ’" The paragraph went on to say:
before and after the service a piper parades the
aisles, skirling an old psalm-tune on his pipes
while all the singers marching in his train unite
their voices in soul-stirring harmony.”
Sydney maidens, no doubt, are now basking in
the gla-nces flashed from alien orbs which lately
warmed the cockles of trusting Melbourne busts.
Xhe fiasana officers spoke English deplorably,
but they progressed like houses on fire in Melb.
ball-rooms. One of them left here an engaged
man, and others are said to have plighted their
troth secretly, preparatory to breaking the news
to der alte mensch which is, I believe. German
for “the old man.”
During their stay, linguists derived enjoyment
from casually-overheard scraps of the conversa-
tions carried on by pur visitors and their partners,
though few of either nationality had any clear
idea of the other s language, they were equally
bold in coming or guessing at words, sometimes
chancing on remarks which made a listener’s hair
stand on end. Like Spettigrew, in “Charley’s
Aunt, they said awful things.
Especially was this the case, I believe, among
the romantic scenery of Healesville, where the
Austrians and a large party of local celebrities
spent a day at the invitation of Fitz-Gibbon the
Great. The able-bodied tars didn’t seem to have
such a good time in Melb. as their officers. At
the Fasaua “at home,” the big men dancing with
the small ones looked as if they found their
partners poor substitutes for the real thing.
All this week Melb, streets have been as well
furnished with bishops as Mark Twain’s ship (in
“Thelnnocents Abroad ”) was with captains. It’s
hard to say which of the dignitaries carries off the
palm for looks, but I believe the head of the
Melb. diocese, although not exactly a beauty, is
the most benevolent of the lot. . Mrs. Goe, at the
recent Ordination show, was the same inoffensive
old body as ever, with her hair skewered up
according to the familiar three-hairpin trick.
Dr. Chalmers preached the ceremonial sermon,
his small, shapely hands evoking much feminine
admiration. Balloon sleeves, when worn by
women, certainly diminish the apparent size of
the waist, but it never struck me before that they
are equally becoming to the hands. The preacher
drew a doleful picture of the new Bishop’s future
career, “Thousands of miles in the lonely bush,
with no companion but his thoughts,” &c., &c.
This might apply to the existence of back-block
parson or itinerant bush-missionary, but not to
that of a bishop, for there is always a squatter or
someone of that sort only too delighted to convoy
the sainted personage wherever he may wish to
go, and sometimes quite a cavalcade shares in the
honour of escorting him.
That marriage of the wealthy young Stawell
squatter to a well-known Melb. belle takes place
shortly; but, unfortunately, the happy man is
suffering from a return of his old complaint, bash-
fulness, and, much to the disappointment of all
concerned, refuses to be “ turned off ” in church.
A wedding of the most private description, and a
honeymoon conducted on lines of the strictest
secrecy, are, he declares, his only chances for
getting through the ordeal at all, and as the
ordeal is the main thing Juliet has sorrowfully
consented to meet his views.
The news of the prospective famine at Ooolgar-
die, or some other circumstance, has warned one
intending emigrant to be wise in time, and so the
artist who was going to open a pie-shop at the
new Eldorado has decided to betake himself
instead to his sunny fatherland. And yet one
would have thought that a famine would be most
favourable for the consumption of pies.
Madame Belle Cole, who is a member of the
Sorosis Club, Was “lionised ” by the Austral
Salon on Monday afternoon. By the way, Miss
Emily Spada, who is also an American, bears so
close a resemblance to Belle that many suppose
them to be sisters.
VAN M.

H. A..Jones’s “Bauble Shop,” staged on
Saturday at Sydney Lyceum, is a powerful drama
which, in some respects, can even bear comparison
with the celebrated “Mrs. Tanqueray.” Jones’s
drama is a burning, blazing satire on the famous
Nonconformist Conscience, of which the dirty
Stead, and Antoinette Sterling, and others of
their kind are the exponents. Hitherto the world
has been swamped with plays wherein the heroine
is betrayed by the cold, sneeriul person in
evening-dress; the “Bauble Shop” is the only
one which this paper remembers in which the
evening-dress person comes to utter grief, and
remains there, principally because his intentions
were honourable—or, at least, as honourable as he
knew how to make them.
•* * ,
Titheradge, who takes the leading part, is
a British Prime Minister with an accidental and
mainly platonic affection for a pretty girl in a
wholesale toy-emporium. His affection is ridi-
culous chiefly because it is platonic ; being unable
to understand himself in his character as a simple,
pastoral kind of lover, he sneaks along in disguise at
dismal hours of the night, and takes weak tea
with the girl and her hiccupy parent, and feels
himself to be a mild, harmless, reformed ass as he
does it. But the baleful eye of the Nonconformist
Conscience is upon him, and one night that hoarse
gorgon stalks in and demands a feasible explana-
tion. There is no feasible explanation handy.
The frantic assurances of everybody concerned
that there is no semblance of guilt about the busi-
ness only elicit a yell of mockery, for the Noncon-
formist Conscience has been there itself and knows
better. The impression left just here is that the
Conscience was probably a gay dog in its younger
days. Next night, in the House, the crash comes,
when the Premier moves the second reading of the
Public Morals Bill, and an avenging Social Purist
replies by telling the story, and' then points him
out with a lean, denunciatory claw, and wants to
know if that is the sort of person to look after
public morals. This scene doesn’t come off
visibly ; the stage is deserted for a while, but the
hoots of the moral majority can be heard from the
back premises like an accusing voice. Then the
Premier falls and never gets up again. There is
no poetic justice in the story ; it ends very much
as it would in real life, and leaves the hero doubled
up beneath an avalanche of execration. Even
when he marries the cause of all this disaster
nobody is convinced of his innocence save himself,
and the principal snufflebuster only sneers a holy
sneer and wipes the dust off his large, flat feet,
and looks like Stead coming simultaneously out of
Sodom and Gomorrah.
* # *
Titheradge, as the Prime Minister, is an
unimpressive politician, but in other respects is so
good that there is little to complain of, Brough,
as the Nonconformist Conscience in the flesh, is a
triumph. He is hard, wooden, gritty, and ex-
pressionless of countenance, with the shaven lip,
and the obtrusive gaol door-mat round the chin,
and the massive frock-coat, which are the recog-
nised outward symbols of the hard - boiled
purity that lies within. Also he has that


DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at
