** * *
George Bignold’s latest melodrama, “ A
King of Crime,” promises to have a long and
prosperous career. It is a robust production, and
there are many people in it who state mysteriously
that they musn’b be seen together; and many
devoted women who hang on desperately to doors
and declare that the minions of the law won’t pass
on any account whatever. Also, it contains a
villain of the most comprehensive description,
who wades through blood and brains and
battered remains, and vanishes through trap-
doors when the law descends upon him ; and a
lost will of the most unburnable character, which
is stolen over and over again, and bought and
sold and cast into fires, yet it turns up all right
for probate in the last act. Likewise, there is a
chaste and gorgeous denouement when the second
villain sits at midnight in a ballroom thinking
over his evil past, and the two bony hands of the
first villain steal out from a curtain behind him,
and strangle him in a cordial and heartfelt
fashion, and then bounce his earthly tenement
upon the floor. The two bony hands behind the
curtain are represented by Albert Marsh, returned
after four years’ absence, who makes about the
most versatile, picturesque, and whole-souled
homicide The Bulletin has seen for a long time.
Even his lightest murder is like the shadow of a
great rock in a thirsty land.
Miss Emma Chambers, who is several
degrees more plump than of yore, returns with
him, and fills a short skirt and two small shoes
most commendably; and Miss Watts-Phillips
represents an intensely pathetic blind woman
with even more than her usual ability. Her
Majesty’s, however, is badly in want of a new
comedian. Frank Harcourt has been promoted
to that billet, vice the departed Sweeney, but Mr.
Harcourt is humorous with violence, and his ten-
der roar is as light and elegant as an anchor,
while Mr. Sterling Whyte, as the assistant
humorist, is about as joyful as the news that a
near relative has died in great agony. If the
humour were altogether cut out of this drama
there would be nothing left to complain of.

That good old slab of grief, the “ Land of
the Living,” filled a night or two at Sydney
Royal during the early part of the week, and con-
ducted itself much as usual. In other words, it
wasn’t more unusual than it usually is, and was a
little better than The Bulletin expected, whereas
this paoer expected that it would be worse than
it expected. Miss Alice Deorwyn was again the
least sombre feature of the show, and the co. was
further brightened by the addition of Miss May
Vivian, who helped the drama considerably on its
uneven way. The Holloway season ends to-
morrow (Friday) with one solitary performance of
Merritt’s ‘‘Broken Idol.”
** * *
Melbourne Bijou re-opened on Saturday.
Arthur Garner had got ready a pious fraud for the
interesting occasion. “The Morals of Mayfair,”
announced by the newspapers as a “popular latter-
day creation,” turns out to be “ The Turn of the
Tide,” by F. 0. Burnand, a play which agitated
England more than 20 years ago, and which was
often seenin Australia before Garner joined the late
triumvirate. Despite the unevenness of construc-
tion, “T. of theT.”isaclever, unconventional work.
Tragedian Bentley almost slaughters the interest
of the hero character. Sweet-voiced, gallant
Henry Neville made it very fascinating in days
of yore, when also Forbes Robertson, a bona-
fide landscape painter, acted the part of the artist
friend very differently to Hans Phillips. Then
the opening of “Turn of the Tide” was a poem ;
now it is uninteresting prose. Ryley, the romantic
poseur, in Bentley’s place, and young Roberts,
from the Royal, instead of spasmodic Phillips,
would entirely change the appearance of the first
act. In some respects the play has never been
better, if as well, acted. Harwood, Annie Taylor
and Miss Watt-Tanner are all placed to a nicety.
Manager Garner shows real feeling through his
hard, dry manner, and holds the audience without
appearing to turn a hair of his superabundant wig.
Miss Edith Blande is perfect in her task, and
Ethel Haydon looks the ingenue, without spoiling
the effect by trying to act beyond her powers.
“The Morals of—er—Playfair ” is prettily
mounted, and it may do good business if the mar-
ried women of Melbourne don’t conspire to call it
“ridiculous.” As a rule, the virtuous married
lady declines to encourage the idea of an Ethel
Haydon waiting to trip into her shoes.
** # *
At Melb. Princess’ the improvement in
Bland Holt’s steeplechase has won a fair triumph
for “The Prodigal Daughter,” The horses begin
to raise something that resembles a gal'op across
the stage, and a jockey, who formerly rolled from
his mount on to the dry boards, now precipitates
himself gingerly into the tank with striking
dramatic effect. Also, Sass’s resolution to wifi
the race or break his neck is expressed with
splendid audacity, and when he takes his call
before the curtain that polished actor pants like a
kitchen bellows, just as though he had been the
real rider of the camel that came in first. “ The
Prodigal Daughter ” seems certain to run over
Saturday.
** * #
“During Her Majesty’s Pleasure,” de-
scribed as an Anglo-West-Australian drama, was
s=t up at the Alexandra on Saturday. One Inigo
Tyrell is the author, and this rampageous bantling
of Inigo’s brain was said to be running loose for
the first time in Australia. Any scheme for
chaining-up the drama might as well include the
capture of the author himself, for he appears in
his own production as a felonious idiot, and his
insane habit of chuckling at the gallery is too
dreadful to dwell upon. “During Her Majesty’s
Pleasure ” includes convictism in both hemispheres,
a repudiated Hindoo wife, a blazing hulk, and a
Sister of Mercy, lively Dora Mostyn being the
emblem of holy spinsterhood. There is likewise
a black object, known as King Koro, the Silent
Watcher of a Gold-mine, who would be useful in
Ooolgardie at the present moment.

“ The Circus Queen ” has positively
decided to appear when the final fortnight of
Maggie Moore’s big season at the Royal com-
raences. “Struck Oil” concludes a seven weeks’
boom to-morrow (Friday), and everyone desirous
of getting a last look at E izibeth Stofel is
requested to grab the opportunity by its tail-tuft
and hang on.
** # *
Madame Belle Cole and her concert com-
pany appear, at the Town Hall to-night (Thurs-
day) for the sixth and last time. As a well-
balanced musical combination they have been
lavishly praised and well supported, but a con-
siderable conflict of opinion rages around each
individual member. The robust and well-trained
tenor is “off” in his high, forced notes, and sings
without much soul, whilst the dramatic soprano is
full of soul and artistic method, without having
any voice worth mentioning. The basso, a success
in some songs, is as dull as Sydney Deane, or a
village sexton, in others. The warmest admirers
of Belle Cole cannot seriously argue that her voice
is great, although it pleases them much, whereas
a good many other people will question whether
she has the best contralto quality of tone. But
taken as a company, and not forgetting the pretty
lady fiddler, they are a highly satisfactory enter-
tainment.

A use for old suburban skating rinks has been found by
a Fitzroy (Melb.) tobacconist. He has organised a
juvenile minstrel co. which plays once a fortnight at each
rink, the admission-charge being “ a silver coin.” Writer
saw over 3000 people at one of these rink-shows a fort-
night back, and at 3d. all round that means £37 10s. for a
one-night show.

her here, and she more than once sang to a £5 house.

Dear Bulletin,— David Christie Murray’s deserted wife
isn’t the Melbourne girl, but his old original wife whom
he left behind when coming to Australia. She protests
against the Melb. mistress with her young son, while
Murray dramatically thanks Providence for sending him a
son in his old age.— Teiciiy,
At the London post-mortem on a “ professional swal-
lower," who used to eat chains, saucers and other solid
articles, a bullet, 30 corks and a string 18in. long were
found in his intestines. The corks were attached to a
leather band which had hooks at the ends, and there were
also 20 pieces of tin-foil in the intestines.
Bland Holt gave £6OO for the Australian rights of “ A
Woman’s Revenge” for seven years. He tried to get
“Charley’s Aunt,” but was outbid by Charles Arnold,
who bought it for Thornton at £I2CO.
No London theatrical m'ahager with any pretensions to
tone now keeps his theatre open during Holy Week.
Time was when Irving was the only one who observed this
pious custom. But actor-managers are big-wigs nowa-
days, and people look to them for good example.
The Pall Mall Gazbttb on actor George Alexander at
a recent theatrical gathering:—“ Debonnair, perfectly
dressed, perfectly groomed, with an exquisite touch of
grey in his hair, wearing a subtle collar of new design—
combining the art of the turndown with a dash of the
smart Westendishness of the upright—and expressing in
his general bearing the noble ideal of the high-class
Englishman of intellectuality tempered with manners.”
Alexander, who left the silk department of a London soft-
goods warehouse to join Irving’s company, has worked
his way into management through “sassiety.” Every
moment that he can snatch from the theatre and bed—
for no man can live Alexander’s life without a reasonable
amount of bed—is devoted to dolling himself up and
paying court to the “swells,” especially “swells” who
may have means and disposition to back up a theatrical
speculation. A clever, superficial, tactful man who has
no time for improving thought, and whose intellectuality
is quite subservient to his subtle collar. Also, a liberal
liquor and chicken provider for all pressmen who can
help to push him along.
The Wild Cat Column,
The 4511 i annual report of the A.M.P. Society is
another record of steady and continuous progress. The
new assurance for the y ar represents £2,869,997, and the
new premium income is over £96,000. The total death
and endowment claims for the year are 1589,000, and
these are more than met by the receipts from interest
alone (which amounts to nearly £659,000), without
trenching upon premium income 1 The funds have been
increased by £626,000, and now amount to a splendid
total of nearly £12,000 000, while the total income is
within a shade of £2,000,000 annually. The expenses have
been further reduced, till they now stand at only 9 per
cent, of the receipts - probably the lowest average in the
world. Over £160,000 has been set aside to provide for
depreciation and to increase the reserves, in view of a
possible reduction in the rate of interest; and even with
this deduction there remains a profit of £448,790 to be
divided among policy-holders. Altogether the A.M.P.’s
report is a pleasant thing to meet with in such times as
the present.
* * * ♦
The Argus refers to “the good impression
created by the satisfactory report of the Colonial Bank,”
whose deposit receipts are offered at about 13s. 6d. in the
£. The old, stupid system of representing each bank in
turn as a marvel of stability is being followed with dreary
persistence by Smellbourne’s press. If a bank were to
declare a heavy loss on the half-year, and own up to the
rottenness of its reconstructed assets, and generally
declare that ruin stared it in the face, the great news-
papers would congratulate their readers upon another
pleasing proof that things were on the mend. But this
eternal song of satisfaction falls on deaf ears. The great
newspapers lied too hard when the “ crisis ” was about.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
John Robb, insolvent, is indebted to the Com-
mercial Bank of Australia for an overdraft of £145,049,
and interest thereon —one of the little matters which
made it “ inadvisable ” to delay the great Commercial
reconstruction scheme in order that an inquest might be
held upon the assets. Another large “secured " creditor
of J. Robb is “ Commissioner of Savings Bank, Melbourne
—£73,131.”

Tooth and Co., the great Sydney beer corpora-

pays a 7 per cent, div., besides adding £2600 to reserve,
and providing for depreciation. Whatever else goes
wrong in Sydney, beer is founded on a rock.

* * * •
Broken Hill Extended crosscut, from the 1330
feet level, the biggest local prospecting work, struck the
lode in the adjacent Central at 263 ft. It is a hopeless
sulphide, which is no improvement on that in the bottom
of Campbell’s shaft, Block 10, equal to 300 ft. above. The
crosscut demonstrates merely that the Broken Hill lode
carries down 1400 ft.—a sorry return for the Extended,
Central, Block 10, and Government (prospecting! grant)
cash expended on it.

Baker’s Creek, 461 tons for 3620 z., a tremendous

♦ * • *
“ Tiger ” Inglis, M.L. A., and another man are
floating the Oremorne coal discovery in England. The
capital is £600,000 and the shareholders are guaranteed
3 percent, on their money while the mine is under develop-
ment. As the mine will yield nothing during the develop-
ment period, this means either that the shareholders are
to get their 3 per cent. div. out of their own capital, or
else that somebody promises to give £15,000 a year out of
his own pocket—the first issue of capital being £500,000.
• • * *
In Mount Lyell, Tasmania has the richest silver
and copper mine in Australia. It has long had the richest
tin-mine in Mount Bischoff, and some people in Sydney,
principally Israelites, think they now have discovered in
Tasmania the boss gold mine of the world. The exact
locality is kept dark; but the writer hears a subdued
whisper about soz. per ton all over the mountain, and
that £25,000 in £250 shares has been put up for develop-
ment, also that two-thirds are retained by the vendors.
Let us hope it is all true. The Jews have been plucky and
persistent gold-mine speculators, and their luck has been
simply horrid. The present scribe knows hundreds of
Hebrews who have piled it in the gold-specs., and he
doesn’t know of one, barring the late Lazarus, of Bendigo,
who ever struck it really rich, or cleared out a heavy
winner.
• • • ♦
Mr. Donovan, late town-clerk of Bendigo, a
straight man of much experience, says there are many
men at Coolgardie who would like to get back to Bendigo.
Then Heaven help Coolgardie !
• * * *
Debate re the goodness or badness of Murchison
goldfield proceeds vigorously in Adelaide papers. One
man writes from Cue to say it’s queer that anybody who
has a show is very anxious to sell it. Another pops up in
Adelaide to point out that prospects are excellent, as all
the leading reefs improve in size with depth ; and once
the railway starts wood will be plentiful, while there is
quite as much water as ever there was at the Barrier. No
doubt it is Westralia’s turn to boom just now, says some-
body else, but we mustn’t forget that there are shows just
as good in North Queensland, spoiling for want of traffic
facilities, and W.A. is a bigger country than N.Q. to cover
with railroads. On the whole, the honours of debate are
pretty easy.
« • * *
Coolgardie has raised the W.A. revenue for
April by 65 per cent., as compared with April, ’93.
Which is about the best Coolgardie item of the week, for
there were but six days’ provisions on the field at latest,
and no water worth mentioning. An exodus from the
field has begun, and the passenger-lists by steamers from
the East show a marked decrease. There is no definite
mining news of importance, and the mistake that
Providence made in not setting a self-filling 400-gallon
tank and a miraculous bag of flour alongside every rood
of auriferous country is the burden of all the intelligence
which a humpless camel with an inflamed stomach brings
irregularly from the scene of inaction.
♦ * * *
“ A very big boom without anything to justify
it ” is the verdict on Coolgardie of J. O Johnston, friend
of Wall, N.S. Wales M.P. Puff, puff, puff—all to get the
money from the East. Far more brought than the gold
got is worth—or will be worth for many a day. Carters
and camel-drivers making fortunes. Poor men starving.
Rich and poor without water. That sums up the opinions
of the ebb-tide now setting away from the rush.
* » * *

gardie as diorite, with large bodies of milk-white, barren-
looking stone, and says the rush will be the most disas-
trous that ever occurred in Australia. Nevertheless,
he believes much English capitaljwill be invested there.

Waiki mine, Maoriland, from Jan. 14 to Deo.
23 last year, crushed 19,805 tons stone for a bullion value
of £61,199 10s. sd.—enabling the co. to pay off £9OOO debt
and distribute £22,500 divs., besides carrying forward a
big sum. This year pan-amalgamation is to be changed
for a Cassel cyanide. Plant equal to treating 2000 tons a
month.
• « * *
Mills’ United g.m. co., Charters Towers, has
paid £140,000 divs. in two years, though the stone has to
be hauled 1600 ft. and does not average an ounce per ton.
Thus, barring Mount Morgan, Mills’ is Australia’s biggest
tonnage and div. gold-mine. In March they broke and
crushed 5600 tons for 58000 z., which, considering that Mt.
Morgan is worked by open cuts, makes Mills’ the boss
mine of the continent for wojk.
• * * *
The writer met two men from Coolgardie the
other day. Both had left the same week, and while one
said there were many heaps of stone which would crush
2oz. to 50z., the other swore there was nothing outside
Bayley’s. Nothing definite and authoritative has yet
reached the East on this vexed question, and some of the
daily-paper representatives are too obviously viewing the
field through Browne-coloured glasses.
* * ♦ *
Queen Constance, Mareeba, Q., made a profit,
last year, of £4281. It never made a call on the original
owners, nor on the capital of the co. Few mines outside
Q. ever performed such a feat.

The latest Vic, coal discovery is at Altona Bay,
where two bores, put down at a mile interval, each struck
a seventy-foot thick seam of brown coal. Altona Bay is a
God-forsaken waste of sand and wild “tomatoes” about
three miles beyond Williamstown, and was probably the
largest advertised of all the boom estates.

• • • *
A correspondent:—Balaclava Hill Co., Whroo
(Vic.), the mine from which a quarter of a million in gold
was taken (the mine which built Menzies’ hotel),..failedlo
float in Melbourne, despite its undeniable attractions.
Everyone is looking Coolgardie-wards just now—caught,
perhaps, by the glitter of the broken-glass on the horizon
and consequently missing the diamond at foot. The
£250,000 worth of gold was all got above 360 ft. and above
water level. Menzies Co. was only a syndicate tribute co.
of 20 shares, with a heavy percentage, and when Manager
Nicholas took charge had spent almost all its capital.
Nicholas stopped the calls, declared many divs., then
expended the reserve and recommended the tribute co.
to wind up, as it hadn’t enopgh money to look below
water level. Hence a mine, proved a great one to com-
paratively shallow sinkings, stands on the market with
itself and a lot of machinery and plant to back the pro-
spectors, and yet the share-list has closed empty.
« • • *
A London weekly quotes an Indian wild-cat
gold mine prospectus: —“ Try and realise what it actually
means—a country where gold has been got to the extent
of hundreds of millions by simply picking it up—a
country whose very history is virtually written in gold.
What may be expected when thoroughly experienced min-
ing engineers get to work in such a land ? Sanguine as
we are, we are absolutely afraid to look into the future,
the prospects are so startling, so gigantic.” It went off
with a bang, and so did the mine—£25,000 was subscribed
and the whole gold obtained might be carried at the end
of a watch-chain. Of course there were several lords on
the Board.

The Bankers’ Institute of Melb. contemplates
forming a big club among the local clerks, and taking the
big building of the now defunct Y.M.C.A. It will he
something on the lines of the popular Melb. Democratic
Club, only, of course, severely “ respectable ’’ and Tory
to the backbone. Meanwhile, this Institute is running
a subsoription-Hst for the assistance of unemployed and
indigent “officers” of various pawn-shops fired out by
the schemes of reconstruction.

fell off.

One Sydney man lias a solid belief in the
might of the Law. He lately took his ne’er-do-
well brother to a solicitor, and, in consideration
of £lO, got him to sign a document agreeing not
to borrow any more money from him.
Dibbs’s contemptuous treatment of Henni-
ker Heaton, over the Canadian Conference affair,
extracted an original aphorism from Alfred Ben-
nett: “It is easier,” said the gifted Alfred, “to
forgive bad politics than bad manners.”
’Enniker ’Eaton’s application to represent
N.S.W. at the Ottawa Conference, “free, gratis,
and for nothing, and all out of his own pocket,”

Archdeacon Maunsell, who died at Auck*
land (M.L.), the other day, has been over 50 year®

Maori grammar. Maunsell, Grey and Dr. Short*

Carington is termed—apotheosis of but-
lerishness !—“ an ideal Lord Chamberlain ” by a
London weekly. He walks backwards with con-
siderable ease, is a diligent student of the laws
and rules of precedence, parts his hair carefully,
and never smiles out of place.
Parkes, on the occasion of his recent
Federation speech at Parramatta, had stipulated
that there should be no demonstration. And
there wasn’t any. The old man walked down to a
Church-street pie-shop and had a scone and a cup
of tea, and then platitudmised.
It is printed that the heir-apparent to the
Duke of Buccleuch won a foot-race at Oxford
recently. That’s right! Every duke should
learn to run. He should also learn to climb
trees an'd dodge half-bricks, and go in for hard
practice in the art of living in caves.
Melbourne Argus has almost ceased to
comment upon the Speight v. Syme case. Its
love for plaintiff is said to have cooled. More-
over, the Age and Argus proprietaries have
shaken hands in private, and agreed to go no
further in a mutually-unprofitable business war.
As witness last Saturday’s issues of the two
papers.
One of Sydney’s wealthiest citizens found
in the street one day lately a wallet stuffed with
notes and coin. In handing it up to the police,
who had been apprised of the loss, it was remarked
that hundreds of hard-up people must have passed
it by without noticing it. Wealth replied :
“Money smells money; I knew I had a good
thing when within five yards of it.”
Shann, accountant of the Bank of Victoria,
Melb., is a humourist in his ponderous, right-
thinking, capitalistic way. The other day a
rather pious clerk came in with a complaint of
abusive language against a fellow employe. “He
called me a blanky fool,” said the aggrieved one
bitterly. But the man of cash didn’t enthuse;
he simply regarded the pile of letters from ruined
shareholders in front of him for a period of s.x
seconds’, and then, rising his head, he inquire 1
benignly : “ Well! Are you? ”

Talmage, the American pulpit-drum
ecclesiastic, with a voice like a bull of Bashan

leaves’Frisco for Australia, May 31. His repu-

of clamorous vociferation.

Poor little Louis Beyers has failed. Holter-
mann, who was a waiter at the European Hotel in
Castlereagh-street, Sydney, went to Hill End with
him as mate, and they made over .£70,090 each put
of their famous claim. Both got into Parliament.
It would almost have been better for the good-
natured, honest Beyers had he got into the debtors’
prison, for in politics he met men who “had”
him for thousands, leaving him stranded in his old
age.

tually never seen S. A. out of Adelaide, but during
the present recess he has much increased his
popularity by travelling to Oodnadatta, 700 miles
north, and Mount Gambier, 300 miles south of
Adelaide. , Apropos , there tis a photo, extant of
Kingston astride a camel, which absolutely refused
to get off its knees as long as the burly Premier

is an expert camel-rider.
John Robb, the Melb. contractor, sets
down his bankruptcy liabilities at £681,505 ;
assets, £448,528 ; deficiency, £232,977. He esti-
mates that within two years prior to sequestration
he lost £532,163—by depreciation in freehold and
leasehold properties he lost £165,209, by deprecia-
tion in the value of shares in trading, tramway,
and other companies, £50,000. He lost £104,112
in connection with a sugar plantation, £101,002
in connection with Talawauta station, and £48,121
on bank-shares.
A London interviewer on Mr. Guy
Boothby, the “intrepid Australian explorer ”: —
A fine, well-built young fellow, with spirits almost
boyish in their quickness and merriment, it was difficult
to realise that only a few months before he had been
battling with death in some of its most hideous forms, in
the lonely wastes which lie hidden away in the centre of
a great and almost unexplored continent.
All the great (advertising) Australian explorers
you meet in London carry gloves, and not one of
the poor things has been long enough across a
horse to become bandy-legged.
There has lately been issued in Sydney a
woman’s paper, “run on American lines,” which
said, last week, that manager Wynne, of the
Daily Telegraph, was a brute, suffering from
swelled head, or words to that effect, and
described how he had frightened into a fainting-
fit a she-reporter attached to the staff of Woman,
who visited his office on a business mission. So
manager Wynne arose in his wrath and found the
woman who was at the back of thelibel, and the man
who was at the back of the woman, and personally
conducted them round to the D.T. premises, in
order that they might point out to him where in
the D.T. office aforesaid the alleged interview had
taken place. The result of the pointing-out being
vague and unsatisfactory, the doughty Wynne
diplomatically got rid of the lady and proceeded
to settle accounts with his reptile contem-
porary. . . . Then Wynne picked up his
friend and put him together again, and
hospitably said, “ Come and wash the blood off in
this basin!” and “Here’s the sponge!” and
“There’s the towel!” and shouted “always glad
to see you ! ” after him as he struck the steps.
The Swiss refuse to disclose their, milk-
condensing trick to S.A. Govt.
Some Coolgardie miners gave a tent-thief
a fright the other day by hanging him up to a tree
with a rope round his neck, and relenting at the
last moment. They didn’t mean business, but
when you’re the man in the noose you never know
what is meant, and your prayqrs are apt to be
sincere. Lynching is an exotic which never throve
in Australia; and if there ever has been a case in
which Judge Lynch really hung a man here, The
Bulletin wants to know about it.

London Spectator, it is cabled, anticipates
another Indian mutiny. Likely enough; once
the wretched natives screw their courage to the
striking-point. Macaulay said the little finger of
the old company was thicker than the loins of
Surajah Dowlah. Were he living now, he might
safely add that a hair of the Government is thicker
than the little finger of the company. Born in
treachery, nourished on tyranny, the English rule
in India is a monument of greed and official
despotism. The country is hopelessly insolvent;
and from a falling revenue and a debased silver
currency has to pay annually 18 millions in gold to
its English proprietors. The natives cannot call
their souls their own, and toil hopelessly for the
support of the governing classes. No wonder
if they should be ready to risk everything on a
bold stroke for freedom.
Among the latest developments of the
eternal Norton-Criek malicious-prosecution case
at Sydney was an attempt by the successful plain-
tiff, Norton, to secure Crick’s arrest, and a
lengthy argument thereon before the Full Court.
For Crick it was pleaded by barrister Wise that
an M.P. couldn’t be arrested while the House was
in session—if this wasn’t the law, then “all the
members might be arrested and legislation brought
to a standstill.” Some people might think that
the sort of legislation likely to proceed from a
Parliament whose members were all in urgent
danger of arrest would be much better brought to
a standstill than otherwise. In fact, Mr. Wise’s
argument ought to knock the privilege idea on
the head, for the theory that the law really pro-
poses to leave such a Parliament at large and to
let it go on legislating is so dreadful as to be next
door to an impossibility.

quack tries to trade on their reputation and
renown. This is the greatest proof of their merit,

reputation and wide-spread use of Clements Tonic

supremacy. Listen to no argument from interested

POLITICAL
POINTS
Prudhomme paraphrased: “ The people of
Australia are free only during general elections.”
The Sydney Land-owners’ Defence League
is “not identified with any political party.” Isn’t
Property a political party ?
The trend of political events indicates
Alfred Deakin as the next Victorian Premier —•
and there will be a new one soon.
N.S.W. Minister for Works has applica-
tions for the expenditure of public money amount-
ing to £25,000,000. Milk the Government!
Labour-leader Cook : “ The Labour mem-
bers have not got such swelled heads that they
are too big for their boots.” Conundrum : Find
Joseph Cook’s brains.
Alfred Deakin’s utterance at Melbourne
last week, emphasising the necessity, first, of
governmental retrenchment, and then of direct
taxation of wealth, particularly unimproved land-
values, marks the trend of current democratic
politics in Australia.
Alfred Deakin : “ Why should 95 mem-
bers of Parliament be required to transact the
business of a million people?” The question
applies to all divisions of the country. Australia
wants fewer politicians and more eyes upon the
doings of each of them.
One election organiser is already nomi-
nated for selection as a parliamentary candidate
for six N.S.W country leagues, and is intriguing
for nomination by a city league. One man one
vote, also one man one Parliament, likewise one
man one colossal cheek.
“ The accursed system under which the
party on this side of the House are always striving
to murder the reputations of the party on the other
side, in order to leap over the dead bodies of their
reputations on to the seats in the Treasury bench.”
—George Higinbotham.
For one Australian-born there are ten out-
siders aspiring for Labour Electoral League nomi-
nation for N.S.W. Parliament. The greatest
disqualification for participation in Australian
affairs, political or municipal, is to be “native
here and to the manner born.”
“ It is my great hope yet to be able to
serve the people of Queensland,’ said Liliey at
Gympie, the other day. The old man, who has
an uncommon v good conceit of himself, will never
be satisfied till he gets even with MTI wraith for
that slap in the face at North Brisbane election.
A Melb. paper writes that the “ unweary-
ing vigilance” of Ebenezer J. Thomas, late
private secretary to the Vic. Premier, prevented
the French from taking the New Hebrides. A
forcible reminder of Sydney Smith’s apothecary,
who sat up whole nights, watching over the
British constitution.
Young Harry Armytage, the squattophile.
member for Grant (Vic.), will not seek re-election,
and both a Chirnside and a Mogg have entered
the lists, thus giving a possible chance for a demo-
cratic candidate.: Armytage is a clever young
fellow, fepPiit by’ environment and interest, for the
natural colour of the man shows up through his
gilded robes in many ways.
Latest news from Samoa shows the' bless-
ings that follow in the wake of rum and
Christianity. Ex-king Mataafa and 13 chiefs are
in exile ;27 more chiefs are starving in gaol; and
the rank-and file of “rebels” have to pay £4OOO
iti fines out of their wretched profits from copra
and cocoanuts. The so-called “ Government ”
has no money, and one set of gaolers, receiving
no wages for nine months, walked off with the
prisoners.

“Please give me a penny !”
The Argus quotes, with approval, the
opinion of The Times that the Welsh Disestab-
lishment Bill is “a gigantic bribe to the rate-
payers, and means the spoliation of the Church.”
The retention of theUhurch as an establishment
is a bribe to the classes and a spoliation of the
people. It is comforting to know that the people
are offered such bribes now and again.

departments, inasmuch as he is Postmaster-
General, Minister for Education, Minister for
Mines, Minister for Works, and Minister for
Railways. Either Wilson is a man of magnificent
industry and abilities, or Ministerial duties
are
Finding that refusal of permission to
establish a distillery at Mildura simply means its
establishment in N.S. W. on the other side of the
border, the Vic. Government are climbing down,
and a licence will probably be granted. Chaffey
Bros, are themselves the applicants. Mildura, as
a teetotal settlement, is a dead failure; and the
success of its sly-grog-shops should point a moral
for all but the most bigoted of un-Ohristian
intemperate fanatics.
The iniquity of Southern legislation in
Queensland is nowhere more apparent than in the
Railway Department. Mt. Perry, a dead-and-
buried Oopperopolis, is connected with its port,
.1 hindaberg, by a most expensive railway. The
Etheridge goldfield, which has produced about
million pounds' worth of gold, a fairly busy centre,
is entirely dependent on horse-team traffic.
“Strange,” say the Southern journals, “the
North is always grumbling.”

of the Soudan Contingent.” This ought to
doubly blast the chances of any candidate, unless
the fact that Shipway was foolish enough to go
t > the Soudan is intended to show that he wasn’t
then raking in ,£2r,00i) a year as a solicitor, and
the fact that he is a solicitor is held to prove
that he was no good in the Soudan.

Then came the usual exposure, followed swiftly

They all do it. Even Deakin—when in

ea.y so.
Some of the evidences of what the
Sydney Telegraph calls “ the great Free-
trade awakening” in Victoria, as ex-
pounded before the Tariff Commission,
were interesting reading. One person,
who had been converted by Max Hirsch’s
lectures, announced : “T am totally op-
posed to Protection, because it makes the
articles I use very dear.” On inquiry, it
appeared the articles in question were
“bags and sacks,” and when the court
pointed out that bags and sacks came in
free, and that it must be Freetrade which
kept up the prices, the witness collapsed.
Another witness said that the only thing
he complained of was the iniquitous duty
on cotton hosiery, and when he learned
that it was also on the free list he fell
back likewise. A third person was against
Protection because it ran up the price of
reapers and binders, and was a wddly-
a i onished witness when he heard that
t,i re was no duty on reapers and binders,
and that the prices were kept up by a ring
of Freetrade importers. These incidents
were mostly dropped out of sight by the
Telegraph, as they looked bad for the
“great Freetrade awakening.”
Labouchere, on the father-on-bench-son-
at-bar system:—“Believing that judges are human,
I believe that these relationships must produce
bias and lead to injustice ; but whether they do or
not, there can be no doubt that they discredit
justice.”
An Australian publisher con-
fesses to have had submitted to him,
since January last, 18 manuscripts,
running from 10,000 to 100,000 words,
dealing with the N.S.W. Parlia-
mentary Labour-party, and some of
them professing to include secret
letters and documents between
members and certain of their sup-
porters.
S.A. Legislative Council elec-
tions for four districts take place this
Saturday (12th). They will be mainly
fought on the question of direct
land-values taxation, as a victory for
the democratic forces, which wou’d
give them a majority in the To,/y
chamber, is much feared by the Ass.
Three district Labour candidates are
being run, and two will almost cer-
tainly get in. Birks, one of the
chosen in the central district, is a
single taxer and will probably, for
that reason, be left out, altho’Adams,
the other Labour candidate, will
nearly head the poll. Several Ass
politicians may become extinct.
Tomkinson, champion of Tories, and
ex-Minister Copley, both the chosen
of the Ass, will probably have a
hard fight. Two other Ass mem-
bers have wisely retired, as defeat
at the hands of the disgusted
workers who elected them was in-
evitable. One excellent feature of the
present contests is the way in which
wealthy candidates have to limit
their expenses in accordance with
the Electoral Expenses Act of ’93,
Glassey will nob have a walk-over fo r
Burke (N.Q.) electorate, on Hoolan’s resignation.
Lissner, ex-Minister for Mines, who lost his seat
at last Charters Towers election, is one opponent;
and Billy Little, ex-any thing you like, is another, on
the ground that he was formerly the best 12st.
man on the Barcoo. Many Etheridge people by no
means relish the idea of making a pocket-borough
for a Southern man j and Labour would have a
much better chance if a good local man could be
found.
Presumably the Melb. May-Day celebra-
tors were refused permission to march in proces-
sion through the streets because they wanted to
do it on a Sunday. The alternative, of course, is
to march on a week-day, anger employers, and
lose wages. Why rational people with good gray
matter in their skulls should cramp their liberties
to the measure of the Sunday fetish The Bulle-
tin fails to see. If it is reasonable and proper to
“procesh” at all, it is just as reasonable on Sun-
day as on any other day.
The N.S.W. Crown appeals now to the
Full Court in almost every case in which a jury
bring in a verdict carrying damages against it.
The Chief Justice, in rejecting the appeal of the
P.M.G. from a decision of a jury-court, which
decreed one Carlaw ,£l5OO for personal injuries
inflicted by a mail-cart, said there was no ground
whatever to sustain an appeal. Yet, in this ease,
the Crown had fea’d Pilcher, Q C., 0. B. Stephen,
Garland and others to fight the appeal. The
grievance is that the country pays for these
tyrannous experiments.
A considerable majority of the Rosebery
Ministry is said to favour a compulsory eight-
hours system in mining labour. John Motley,
Chief Sec. for Ireland, figures as leading spokes-
man for the minority. Among private members
who declare that an eight-hours working day will
prove a “ death-blow ” to English industries is the
alleged Radical member for the coal-division of
Northumberland. If colliers will vote for members
of the mine-owning interest they must expect to
be sold by their representative whenever their own
interests are immediately concerned. However,
the Bill seems to be as good as through the Com-
mons, and the Peers will assuredly cast.it forth in
disgust. After which —what? Socialistic legisla-
tion has commenced, and the fate of the House of
Lords is fast coming to a crisis,
Mildura (Vic.), the Chaffey teetotal irri-
gation settlement, is appealing to be allowed to
erect a distillery. One of the Chatfeys promises
MADAME LUCIEN HENRY.
the Government that, though Mildura may make
brandy, it will not drink it. Chaffey must be a
s.ingmni promisor of impossibilities. Further,
the humour of a “teetotal settlement” manufac-
turing the abhorred thing, in order to poison off
foreign parts, does not seem to have entered
into the money-making mind. By all means,
let Mildura have its distillery. Likewise
a brewery —“ merely for export.” Many peaceful
Kerristians export arms to the poor heathen;
many lanky teetotallers send rum to savages over
the seas. What a world it is !
An interesting sequel to Premier Patter-
son’s row with the Age as to his remarks on the
fiscal policy at Williamstown has just transpired.
Patterson averred that he condemned Prohibition,
and the Argus backed him up, but the Age in-
sisted that “Protection ” was the word used. It
happened that a short-hand writer of the Works
Department was at the meeting in his private
capacity and stenographed the speech. A copy of
his transcription was forwarded to the meat-man,
whose joy knew no bounds on finding that it cor-
responded with his own version. And now, by
the grace of God, that astute youth has been pro-
moted to the position of shorthand-writer and
private secretary to the Premier.
Sydney Trades and Labour Council (repre-
senting chiefly metropolitan labour unions) has
resolved to disband and re-form as a district
council under the Australasian Federation of
Labour, according to the scheme adopted at Bal-
larat 1891 congress. Only one union directly
opposed the change, though several held aloo .
The debate went to show that the “old unionism ”
which considers unions merely as wage-raising
and trade-improving agencies, has still a con-
siderable following among the older workmen,
who dread the active political effort which is the
“new unionism’s” leading feature. But the
great weight of opinion held that labour must
adapt itself to the new conditions if it ever hopes
to enforce its wishes.

N.S.W., Premier Dibbs’ advice to the
business-man anxious -to enter politics may bring
to mind Sargent, a worthy schoolmaster, who
headed a recent important deputation to the Vie.
Minister of Education. Years ago, he was bitten
with an uncontrollable desire to immolate himself
on the shrine of his country. Despite his large
family, he resigned, got into Parliament, was dis-
illusionised amid the “knaves and fools,” and
counted himself lucky to begin again some rungs
down'the ladder.

MILDURA TEETOTAL SETTLEMENT.
«After all, it appears, arrangements have
been, made with the N.S. Wales Government for
a license to distil in that province, just across the
river from Mildura. This will enable
residents of Mildura to have their
grapes made into brandy, which
will pay etcise-duty to N.S.W.

:v.
NEW MTLDT7RA SCHEME.
Explanations:—R, Murray River; N, New South
Wales side; V, Victoria side; I), Distillery ; G, Boatload of
grapes from Victoria to be made into brandy at distillery
(D) ; P.P., Pipes (made water-tight to keep the Murray out
of the brandy) acting as a syphon
from distillery (D) £o tank (T); P, Distributing Machinery;
C, Consumer; E, Excise Officer.

about equally ‘between astronomy and private bars, tells
its readers that “it is not generally known that at
present Venus may be distinctly seen in the daytime
by those who know where to seek for her.”

Gov. Duff is getting himself disliked. A
Richmond man, the other day, proposed a vote of
censure on him for refusing to open the Hawkes-
bury Agricultural Show; and added that he’d
never get the chance again. The time is coming
when a repentant vicegerent’s “Please may I open
your show ?” will be Contemptuously sniffed at by
every ploughman in the province.
# * *
The fair Mayor of Onehunga (Maoriland) states that in
her capacity of Justice of the Peace she refused to attend
in an affiliation case, from purely conscientious motives.
“She thinks that in such cases a right-minded woman is
inclined to be harder o.i her sex than men.”
Debate in N.S.
Wales Assembly re
the local policeman’s
pistol, showed the
hazy state of public
opinion on the sub-
ject. G. H. Reid
and others thought
police should be
armed at night, and
not by day. Why ?
In daylight they
■can at least see what
they are shooting
at; at night they
■cannot, and, what
is of more conse-
quence, even if the
■object to be fired at
is visible, it is im-
possible to take aim
•except in bright
moonlight. Any
shooting done at
night must be of
the wildest, and far
more dangerous to
the general public
than to anyone else.
An English poacher
going pheasant-
shooting at night
strikes a chalk-line
along his gun-barrel
to guide his eye to
the sight. If the
police are to carry
a more efficient
weapon than a
baton, let it be a

Melbournian: How horrible the fate of the
unhappy M'Dougall, engineer, who suicided the
other evening! Xhree mouths he, an honest,
sober man, had wearily sought work, and the
family had got down to a little bit of sago, on
which they had to exist all Sunday. Ihe joy-
bells of the churches mingling in his ears with the
cries of his hungry children, so he died. Another
text for Chief-Justice Madden, on the cowardice
of the broken breadwinner.
Yet the Rev. Alexander, comfortably, to
Melb. Presbytery : —“lt is at any rate satisfactory
to know that there is no risk of anyone starving
provided he is willing to work.” The Rev.
Alexander should re-fashion his cheerful opinion :
“It is satisfactory to know that no one need die
of starvation provided he is willing to hang him-
self.”

The question, who are to possess at hospital-
meetings the votes acquired by the .£l7OO raised
on Sydney Hospital Saturday, admits of length-
ened argument. The Herald takes the side of
the organisers and collectors, contending almost
in Ouida’s words that good actions require to be
manured with the hope of a little profit of some
kind ; but if this principle be accepted, and ,£1
collected procure an equal voting-power with .£1
subscribed, an occasion may arise on which the
government of public institutions may be con-
trolled by collectors alone.

Editor Bulletin, —During a recent N.S.
Wales Circuit sittings a septuagenarian juror, the
unfortunate possessorof a pronouncedly cancerous
lip, kissed the book with the utmost gusto. As
rational beings, cannot our civilisation devise a
more cleanly and at the same time equally binding
method of taking (sic) “ the oath ?” I, for one,
object to touching pages slobbered over by every
cancerous and scrofulous subject whom the law
may decide to swear. I may add that the Sheriff,
on the matter being brought to his notice, caused
the book to be destroyed.— Juror, April 28, ’94.

The cabletnan wires from London at great
expense that doubts are entertained re the sanity
of an English parson, one Tollemache, who stops
occasionally in the midst of the service to blow a
policeman’s whistle. How Tollemache’s insanity
affects Australia doesn’t appear. It is an awful
thing to have telegrams sent from the furthest end
of the earth to say that an utterly unknown sin-
shifter blows a policeman’s whistle.

the further development of friendliness between
Jews and Gentiles.

Dear Bulletin, —Of all mean men I think
the meanest is the man (perhaps it is a woman)
who regularly steals flowers and plants from the
graves in Waverley (Syd.) cemetery. Our tribute
to the dead we love is pitiful, foolish, if you will !
but if you knew the tears with which we water
the tiny clump of violets, or the rose-bush bought
by self-denial, you would understand the pang
with which we find thorn uprooted and stolen.
Even savages respect the graves of their enemies,

“Half-a-million Chinamen die every month
without God,” said a missionary in Victoria the
other day. In other words, according to the good
old missionary logic, six million Chinamen go to
sheol annually. The Chinese are a very, very old
nation, which seems to have started in the early
dawning of the first day, and, at the very lowest
estimate, this process has probably been going on
for 5000 years. This means that there are now
thirty thousand million Chinamen in sheol. and
they still keep coming at the rate of 500,000 a
month. There is an endless stream of Chinamen
pouring, like Niagara, down the sliddery slope
which leads to the final drop—an endless, cease-
less torrent of dead men with pigtails flying
behind them —a howling blast of yellow ghosts,
screaming in strange, angular characters, and
trying to brace their fleshless feet against nothing
to stop their descent. Perdition, in short, is a
place that is full of Chinamen, which lends a new
horror to the hereafter, and adds a new odour to
the bottomless halls of nights This is an im-
pressive view of the case, and one which has
not received the attention it deserves. The
material horrors of Gehenna have been worn
threadbare, but the fact that Gehenna, is full of
Chinamen lias not been impressed on tire public
Miss INEZ BENSUSAN.
mind as it ought to be. Yet, as an incentive to
piety, it should be of immense value; there-
fore The Bulletin suggests that this great truth
should be printed in huge gilt letters above every
pulpit, where all the congregation can see it. T hmk
of the impressiveness of it: “ Brethren, Hell is
full of Chinamen !”

* # •
A young man named Mathieson shot him-
self last week at Eaglehawk (Vic.), the presumable
reason being a difficulty about the furniture. His
marriage was fixed for a date just a week ahead,
and he was trying to borrow the money for the
tables and chairs. He didn’t get the money, and
finding that he and his wife would have to take
their meals on the floor, he went and perforated
himself with a gun, after scattering the wedding
presents around on the grass. This item could be
turned into a good “ad.” for some enterprising
time-payment furniture establishment.

Melbourne burglars carried away two
dozen cane-bottomed chairs out of a kirk, last
week. A “judgment” on luxury—a proper
reward for the heresy of abandoning the old back-
ache, keep-awake, Puritan pew. No “ fence
would purchase pews, even as firewood.
» # *
Rev. Geo. Walters, Sydney Unitarian
minister, writes about “ the ruin brought by
that cold-blooded piety which masquerades in the
garb of Christianity,” This is apropos of “ Mrs.
Tanqperay,” which the rev. gentleman decides to
contain “ a lesson of wisdom, charity, and virtue.”
The virtue, of course, is summed up in Ellean —
“ Saint Ellean,” the pious prude who is so shocked
at naughty mamma-in-law’s going’s-on. The
Bulletin is privately of opinion that of the two
Ellean would be far the worse to live with.
Ellean, the young woman with a call to grace,
wears a kill-joy countenance, looks at
light conversation, and obtrudes her “soul at
every possible opportunity. There are few tilings
more frightful than the female saint. Woman is
naturally a sinner, and the more she sins, within
due bounds, the better company she is.

Dear Bulletin, —Sappho Smith says the
naval officer isn’t what he was. It’s a case of
evolution. The “sea-dog” was damnable ; the
“sea-dude” is despicable; the transition animal
was the fine fellow whose decay S.S. deplores.—A.

Dear Bulletin, — On Hospital Saturday
night, in a Sydney shadowed way, one young
woman said, “ Ullo, dear, w’ere yah goin’ ? Come
’ere to me, dear ! ” Ten yards farther on another
young woman rattled a box on my chest, “ Oh, do
give me something, please.” Both used the same
weapons for a similar object. Each appealed to
my sex-instinct in the hope of securing my money.
Yet, in the eyes of society, one is vicious, the
other virtuous. Why?— Cholk.

ain’t here neither, yer Worship.” His Worship :
“ Who is your lawyer ? ” Plaintiff i ‘‘ Mr. Blank,
your Worship.” Defendant: “Yes, yer Worship,
he’s mine, too.” The lawyer had accepted fees
from both sides and did not appear for either.

A Melb. Hebrew bridegroom, who was
jilted on the morning of the wedding, a few years
back, did not blow his brains out. He simply
strolled around Collins-street, collected a couple
of dozen of his friends, and adjourned to the
Vienna Cafe, where much fizz was uncorked in
honour of his lucky escape.

* * *
The latest foolishness is a proposal, now
under way in Sydney, for the formation of an
“Association of Australians.” The objects of
this gathering are “to promote social feeling
among the members, and to improve them politi-
cally and intellectually,” and the persons eligible
for membership are “ Australia!?" natives, those
who have resided 10 years in the colonies,
those who came to the colonies before the
age of 14 years, and any who may be
admitted by a two-thirds majority of mem-
bers.” Therefore, the Association is prepared, in
a general way, to admit everybody. It is only the
Australian who makes such a complete and
perfect and comprehensive exhibition of himself
as this. Anywhere else a man who tried to get
up a society where the only qualification
for membership was that every person
must be himself and be born in his own
country, and where the only visible object was to
celebrate the fact that every member was himself
and had been born in the land that he belonged
to, would arouse a general howl of derision. But
the Australian takes such things seriously. He is
so profoundly astonished to find that he belongs
to his own country that he must organise himself
and celebrate the event, or burst. In most cases
it is a great pity that he doesn’t burst.
* * *
One sanguine parson writes, suggesting
that the A.N.A. should start a fund to augment
the salary of the recently-appointed Bishop of
Grafton, who happens to be native-born. The
A.N.A. has done several foolish things, but surely
it won't, in these days of hunger and misery, set
about cadging money for the possessor of a snug
income of .£550 per annum.

owner writes indignantly to the Argus about cer-
tain street women who accost him on his road,
and attempt to steal his honour. He proudly
signs his full name and address. What an adver-
tisement of virtue ! What a humorist!
THE RIGHT MINDED WOMAN.
The right-minded woman is harder than men
(Whose natures are coarser, it may be)
In judging unlicensed maternity when
It creeps into Court with the baby.
She dares at the “creature” caressing her “brat,”
Weak offspring of sentiment tender ;
The right-minded woman knows nothing of that —
It is not in her feminine gender.
“Temptation’s ” a word which she cannot allow
As a decent excuse for an error ;
The mention of “vice” brings a frown to her
brow —
O, the right-minded wife is a terror !
Don’t whisper the platitude, “Passion is blind,”
In fruitless endeavour to bend her ;
For Propriety rules her connubial mind,
And bosses her feminine gender.
And Virtue is not a “comparative term,”
On that she is very decided ;
All girls upon earth can be equally “ firm ”
Woe to whom woe hath betided !
She seldom went out in her warmer young days
Without dear mamma to attend her ;
She was painfully modest in manners and ways,
As befitted her feminine gender.
’Tis perfectly proper for virgins to wed
In a chapel, a church, or a minster ;
There Midas, who hasn’t a tooth in his head,
May purchase a shuddering spinster.
Her marriage was made in the regions above;
Though his lawful embraces offend her,
Her baby's a token of virtuous love
And a proof of her feminine gender.
Woman, as sung by the amorous bard,
Belongs to a mythic Arcady,
Where, suckled by Venus, she never is hard,
Except on “the right-minded lady.”
Instructed by Nature she sighs in the arms
Of Strephon, who lives to defend her,
And Love is the lord of those bountiful charms
That glorify feminine gender.

Another aspect of the “ thrift ” fetish. In
Melbourne sewing-shops scores of girls whose
parents are “well-to-do” work at nominal rates
for “pocket-money,” driving their poorer sisters,
who sew for a living, to slave for similar rates—or
starve. Many working girls club together, co-
operatively rent a large room to live in, and exist
on tea and bread and butter as the only alterna-
tive to the streets.

12.5 p.m.—A boy selling sandwiches.

I.2o.—Keidy—had I a fiver to spare “ till Saturday."

No cheques.)

Early closing on Saturdays will soon be
abolished in Adelaide. The shopkeepers never
willingly swallowed it, and now urge that the
suburbs are taking trade which they lose by
closing early. The working man’s wife will shop
kite.
• * •
At Newcastle (N.S.W.) lately the police
arrested for drunkenness a respectable resident—
who died in the cell without medical aid. After
post-mortem a surgeon swore that death occurred
from apoplexy caused by the rupture of a large
bhiO l-vessel in the back of the brain ; there were
no tracts of alcohol. Which ghastly item needs no
comment.

* * *
At Melbourne, the other day, a mere ser-
vant, an uninteresting daughter of the lower
orders, aged 17, in a wretched dress, had the inso-
lence to plead kleptomania as an excuse for stealing
goods valued 9s. from her employer, “ I was
wicked and couldn’t help it,” she said. The police
asserted that the girl simply buried every thing she
stole, and seemed to forget all about it —but she
went to gaol all the same. Kleptomania, like
gout and papal indulgences, is the glorious privi-
lege of the well-to-do.

The girl-woman gang is disappearing from
Melbourne. Her parents have doubtless im-
pressed upon her the necessity of letting her skirts
down and tying her hair up to catch a husband
before it is too late.
• * #
At Bendigo, the other day, a man offered
a reward of 40s. for the recovery of a lost diamond,
valued at ,£45, and a few hours later a pie-man
called, and persuasively handed to him the missing
gem, and collected the reward. A little later on
it transpired that the supposed gem was a piece of
wiridow-glass from an earring found in a penny
prize packet, and the enraged owner is now sueing
the pie-man for the 40s. The name of the enraged
owner is Levy, which is the most astonishing
feature in the story. It isn’t one person in a
billion who can get 40s. from a person named Levy
for a piece of window-glass.

An old army-sergeant in an Australian
town sent the following letter, the other day, to
his ex-captain, concerning whose utter stony-
brokenness he had just heard : “ Dear Captain,—
I have got a billet as barman at the Thrumbar
Hotel. Come and drink the bar dry. I am on
from 10 to 7 mostly.—Yours,” &c. The Captain
and another deadbeat took a run round without
a thrum, walked jauntly in, and —a barmaid
popped up ! That incident is life epitomised.

# # #
A Presbyterian clergyman was, with a
friend, arrested last Saturday in Sydney, for
stealing a carrier’s cart and horse. In tiiis wise.
They met outside a shop, while the vehicle was
alongside the kerb. The layman needed some
speedy service; the parson agreed—“ Yes, we’ll
go there at once, if you’ll drive.” On alighting
to enter a hotel—“ Will he stand?” quoth the
parson. “Stand? Aren’t 'they your horse and
trap?” “Mine? No. Aren’t they yours?”
“No; I thought it was your turn-out.” “I
thought it was yours.” Then there was a scurry
back, but the owner had them arrested cn route,
and the affair had to be untangled before solemn
and suspicious policemen.
# # *
At Cranbourne (Vic.), writes a corres-
pondent, the outside of the R.C. chapel is painted
orange and green —which striking combination
has a history. Years back the chapel needed a
coat of paint, and for the wherewithal the priest
vainly appealed to his congregation. His rever-
ence thereupon appealed to outsiders chiefly, and
a Presbyterian and Anglican contributed fully
two-thirds of the amount required. To recognise
their generosity, and at the same time expose his
niggardly flock, the priest ordered that the
heretics be represented in the paint by orange and
Catholics by green. And despite the protestations
of his people, who offered to pay the cost of paint-
ing out the objectionable colour, he refused to
allow it.
A saintly deputation, with a look of holy
horror in its eye, waited upon the Vic. acting-
Minister of Defence the other day, to snuffle about
the unrighteous conduct of the Vic. warriors on
Easter Sunday at the encampment. The local
Tommy Atkins, it appears, “whooped it up” on
the “ Board’s ” day, and went in for mixed drinks
and comic songs. “ Instead of mortifying their
flesh,” said smileless Balfour, “they forced the
young cadets in camp to witness the Sunday
turned into a day of revelry.” What a humourless
incubus is the respectable saint! The State,
celebrates the passing to Paradise of the Saviour,
whose mission was to spread peace and brotherly
love, by exercising its trained man-killers in the
work of cutting throats and blowing their brethren
into charred fragments, and the modern disciple,
with a red nose and a gouty toe, finds nothing to
wail about beyond the fact that the beastly common
soldiers sang “ Killaloe ” on the blessed “Saw-
buth.”
Mr. R. Innes, of 14 Castlereagh street,
Sydney, sends round a price-list of N.S.W. wines-
It is a beautiful list, and even the sight of it
raises a great, yearning thirst in the journalistic
soul. There are on it such items as “rich old
verdeilho,” at 245. a dozen ; madeira with a “nutty
flavour,” “superior old hock,” and “full-bodied
burgundy.” Also, there- is a profoundly soulful
item which reads “Frontignac, six years old,”
and which sounds like a dream of Paradise—
that kind of Paradise which comes along in a bottle
with the name on the cork as a guarantee against
imitations. These wines are better and purer than
the European product costing twice the money; they
are vastly cheaper because they haven’t paid import
duties half round the earth, and the charges of a
dozen middlemen. Also, they are prepared in a
civilised fashion, and don’t come from those wildly
poetic lands where the Ouida-esque native presses
the grapes with his feet.
Messrs. Stewart Dawson and Co., the
largest jewellery firrn in the Southern hemisphere,
issue an attractive catalogue, showing designs and
prices of a few among their innumerable articles of
trade. Stewart Dawson and Co. are themselves
the manufacturers, the w holesale dealers, importers
and retail salesmen, and, as they thus save all the
intermediate profits of the trade, they are able to
sell to the public at ordinary wholesale prices,
and yet supply goods of the very highest
quality. In addition to their headquarters in
Britain, they have large establishments in Sydney,
Melbourne and Auckland, and their stock in-
cludes everything even remotely connected with
the jewellery business. Their watches, in par-
ticular, have a world-wide reputation, and range
from a first-class timekeeper at 18s. 6d. up to the
most gorgeous and elaborate structure yet in-
vented. Gold watches are quoted as low as £5
10s., and the chronometer which is usually sold at
,£3O, is offered by them at £2O. All their goods,
in fact, are at 33 to 50 per cent, below ordinary
rates, the difference representingthesavingeffected
through dispensing with the obtrusive middleman.
The catalogue further embraces silver bracelets,
brooches, and bangles, gold ditto, either in plain
metal or set with all varieties of precious stones,
stars, crescents and miscellaneous ornaments, gem
rings, wedding, keeper and other rings, gold and
silver alberts, lockets and appendages, ladies’
chains, sleeve-links, studs, either of the ordinary
kind or with ingenious contrivances to counteract
the fiendish malevolence of the common or garden
stud which persists in flying off and concealing
itself behind the chest of drawers; pins, purses,
pencil-cases, medals, card-cases and sovereign-
cases, which latter are an elegant and beauti-
ful mockery at a time when everybody’s
sovereigns are reconstructed in some. bank
or other. And, in addition to all this,
there is a vast collection of clocks, silver-
plate, electro-plate, toilet-articles, tea and coffee
services, cruets, claret-jugs, spirit-stands, cups,
opera-glasses and the like. The firm is especially
strong in the matter of clocks, and turns out
beautiful articles in marble which are wonders of
elegance and cheapness. Also, a large collection
of unset precious stones is kept on hand for the
benefit of those who prefer to have jewellery made
upon their own designs. The firm’s Sydney
branch in George-street, at the corner of the
Strand, is in itself a sufficient evidence of the
lavish scale on which its operations are con-
ducted.
Dear Bulletin, —lie query about disabled
Chinamen : A wooden-legged Chow keeps a fruit-
shop at Fremantle, W.A. The chief reason why
he is rare in Australia is the old black-sheep
reason : there are not so many Chinamen to get
disabled. And, as a rule, when one is badly hurt,
he either kills himself, or his mates kill him, or
else they coddle him (they are generally very good
to their sick). In any case, he disappears from
public life. —Ben Jemmy.
Disinterested advice to parents. If your
son in his tender years evinces a desire to smoke,
and thus risks to stop short in his growth, it may
not al ways be wise to thrash him till he makes up.
his mind to knock off. In most cases an excellent,
remedy is an old pipe, made sour with long experi-
ence, and administered without pity but with very
strong tobacco. Above all things, avoid to let
him ever get a taste of a Frossard’s Swiss cigar,,
for then he will never, never give it up.
A GOOD STOVE.
A WORD OF CAUTION.
Everyone knows what a good stove is—at least,
the women do ; and every woman knows that, of
all the treasures of the household, a really first-
rate, reliable cooking-stove is the greatest desider-
atum in a house. A great many stoves are
advertised every year, many of which are like
jerry-built houses—thrown together to sell. A
word of caution is therefore necessary to intending
buyers, because there are few things so annoying
and vexatious as to buy a stove and then find that
it does not possess all the qualities that are neces-
sary to give complete and lasting satisfaction.
Now, the points in choosing a good stove are:—
1. Strong cast-iron frame.
2. Good draught.
3. Facilities to burn either wood, coal, or
even turf.
4. Simplicity of parts, and thus, easy to clean.
5. Duplicate parts kept in stock, so that
bxeakages can be replaced at small cost
and without delay.
And if, in addition to these five points, you can
get a stove that is also an ornament to the kitchen,
all the better.
Price, 6f course, is a point, but that is a matter
which need scarcely be mentioned, since, if the
.above points are combined in any stove, it would
be cheap at any price within reason.
It only remains for us to say that, with con-
fidence, we can honestly recommend “The
Housewife,” sold by Holdsworth, Macpherson
and Co., 250 George-street, Sydney, from ,£3 10s.
upwards, as one that fully and entirely “ fills the
bill.”
We should suggest our readers writing to this
firm for an illustrated catalogue, with further
particulars.

A MS. PICKED UP ON SHARK ISLAND.
The Efficacy of Local Option.
The Local Optionists, of N.S.W. especially, are>
making elaborate preparations for securing a
larger legislative representation at the next
election, in order to bring Parliament to. the con-
sideration of the liquor traffic. It is exceedingly
doubtful if these measures will have any ap-
preciable effect in decreasing the Jong roll of
drunkards, who are being increased and mariu-
tured every day. The drunk, as a rule, does
not drink because of the barmaid, or because of
the company he is in. He drinks because the
peculiar wearing effects of life in a civilisation
have caused the desire for the alcohol which was
a disgusting vice yesterday and is an absolute
indispensability to-day. The capacity of the
average man as a consumer of alcohol is, say the
pathologists, about 6ozs. All above that quantity
stays in the stomach in its crude state. It rages
most fiercely on the grey matter of the brain, and
its continued use so hardens the nerve centres that
they refuse to perform their functions without the
aid of a fresh stimulant. Then comes the craving
for the morning dram to steady the nerves, make
an artificial appetite, and tone the flaccid muscles.
Religious sentiment objects to our condoning the
liquor habit by calling it a disease and not a
crime. Once it was a crime, now strengthened by
time it is disease and crime both. In the majority
of cases all the resolutions in the world; all the
signing of pledges, all the struggles of weak
nature against the old habit are useless unless
backed up by such a material help as Warner’s
Safe Cure. Safe Cure and resolution will abso-
lutely destroy the liquor habit. That has been,
proved in numberless instances. ,
Mr. William Patterson, of Cairns, Queensland’,,
writes thus:—“l commenced the use of your
Safe Cure some years ago when it was first intro-
duced into the colonies. I was a very heavy-
drinker in my early days, and with the excessive-
use of spirits my brain became softened and ■my
reason nearly gone. I became alarmed at the-
state I was in, and would gladly have taken any-
thing that I thought would do me any good. I
had a wife and family to support and was in a
fair way of business, but could not attend to it as
.formerly. Reading one day something about
Warner’s Safe Cure, I determined to give it a
trial. I managed, after some difficulty, to pro-
cure a few bottles and commenced its use, keeping
strictly to the directions and not taking any
strong drink in the meantime. After using a few
bottles my brain became clear and I felt altogether
a new man.”


A. W. STEPHEN, L.S.,
HYDRAULIC ENGINEER.

OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
Ih the matter of the application of James Walter Grimshaw, mortgagee under memorandum of mort-
gage from Charles Edwin Goodwin, No. 183,904, dated Bth August, 1891, over the whole of the
land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 492, folio 18.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagee has made application for an order of foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts,
as required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised in the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will remain
open for sale until the 15th June, 1894, which date has been appointed as the time upon or after which
I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicant an order for foreclosure, which will exclude all
claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any other person claiming through or under him, unless
in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy the principal
apd interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest up to
the time of such sale.
A plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office,
Elizabeth-Street, Sydney.

Weatralian Works Commissioner Venn,
acting in compliance with a trades-petitlon, re-
minds Government contractors that " sub-
letting ” will not be allowed.
The commercial editor of Sydney Tele-
graph remarks, in the financial column, that it is
only the apathy of N.S. Wales which retards
Federation. The commercial editor, having
nothing to do with politics and being under no
obligation to boost the Foreign-trade interest,
thus makes a ghastly exhibition of the other
editor, who is busy all the time pointing out that
it is the Victorian tariff which retards Federation.
This recalls another episode of seven years ago,
when the Telegraph’s previous commercial editor
announced that the Parkes Government was going
to take the protective duty off candles, and this,
it was confidently expected, would check local
production, and thereby raise prices. All the rest
of the paper was busy declaring, at the top of
its voice, that Freetrade lowered prices while Pro-
tection raised them, and this unlooked-for candour
struck the editorial department like a cold door-
key on the spine.
At Mackay(N.Q.), three years ago, a kanaka
was suspected of leprosy, examined by the local
medico, and discharged. Recently the police
suspected some more, so the black was arrested,
and a chunk of alleged leprosy was scraped off
his person and posted to Brisbane for examination,
the Government medical officer being quite unable
to visit so far-away a place as Mackay. The
sample was duly reported to be leprous, and
without being seen by the doctor who decided his
fate, the kanaka was shipped off to the leper
dep6t at Friday Island, Torres Straits. Comment
was met by the remark, “Oh, it’s only a kanaka.”
Furthermore, it’s only North Queensland, which
should be very well pleased to be governed as a
Queen-street bureaucracy sees fit. But what price
the medical authorities who award life-in-death
without seeing the patient, in such a complicated
disease as leprosy; and what price the liberty of
the subject in a land where the Govt, acts at once
on such an off-hand dictum ?
M'Burney, Wallis & Co.'s Report.
Messrs. M'Burney, Wallis and Co. report having held
usual fortnightly sale to-day, when they submitted a cata-
logue comprising chiefly country scoured parcels, together
with station odd bales in the grease. The usual number of
buyers were in attendance. We found the market slightly
easier as compared with our last auction, being more espe-
cially noticeable in the case of inferior wool; though for all
classes it was evident that buyers’ limits were to some ex-
tent reduced ; at all events, no great desire was evinced to
do business at prices lately ruling. The cabled informa-
tion to hand yesterday in regard to the opening of the
London May sales, reporting a downward tendency in
the market there, militated without doubt to a great
extent against satisfactory sales being made here to-day.
Shippers last season, we are afraid, will not have much
cause for satisfaction should they compare notes with
neighbours who realised in Sydney market. Some of
the principal lots sold by us were:—WH over Y,
9 bales scouted fleece from Tumut at IOJd. per lb., and 2 of
bellies and piece at BJd.; Riverside over JNM over Dubbo,
6 scoured first clothing at 9Jd., 3 third skin at 7d., 1 at 5Jd.;
T. Sorrell over Bolairo, from Monaro, 1 scoured crossbred
at 7Jd, lat 7d; 1 bale, TJ over New England, 1 scoured
lambs at BJd; 1 bale, HC&C, from Forbes, at7|d; PR,
from Tumut, 3 scoured fleeoe at 9d, 3 pieces at 7|d, 1
lambs at 6d, 2 locks at 6d ; from Walcha, WW, 2 washed
at BJd, 1 at 7Jd, 1 at 7d ; J. Cox over Mangopolah, 2
greasy at BJd; HCR, 1 greasy mixed at Bd; JT over A over
New England, 1 greasy pieces from Uralla at 6Jd, 1 locks
at 6Jd perib.

The Clifton Distribution.
DARLING DOWNS, QUEENSLAND.
THE winner ot the first prize in the Clifton
Distribution will obtain a large fortune and
be set up for life, the prize being the famous
Clifton Head Station, with 4208 acres
magnificent land, the finest on the Darling Downs
two capital houses, cottages, stores, stabling;
horse, cattle, sheep and cow yards; gardens,
cultivation 30 acres; 7000 sheep, horses and cattle.
The land is divided into six paddocks ; splendid
water supply ; 2 miles from Clifton township and
railway station, and 61 hours journey from Bris-
bane. Valuation, £21,400 or £17,000 net cash.
The second to the ninth prizes vary in value from
£4460 to £1096 , and are grand and valuable prizes
from which a large return can be obtained. The
tenth to eighteenth prizes are from £9BB to £225,
and would form beautiful homesteads. The
nineteenth to thirty-sixth prizes ate farms within a
quarter of a mile of Nobby Railway Station, every
acre ready for the plough ; value £5O each. The
thirty-seventh to three hundred and fifty-sixth prizes
value £5 to £2O each can be sold for cash less 10
per cent, by the winners. The agents guarantee
those amounts. So long as the subscriptions cover
the value of the first pnze, it will be drawn for
with other prizes; to be publicly announced before
the drawing. The drawing will be a public one, on
Tattersall's system; due notice will be given of
time and place; all shareholders admitted. The
agents also desire to say that the committee —
consisting of the Hon. J. O. Heussler, M.L.C.;
Arthur Morgan, Esq., M.L.A.; Thos. Alford,
Esq., J.P.— are gentlemen of high standing in the
community. Our solicitors (Messrs. Thynne and
Macartney), and our auditors (Messrs. Turner and
Strong) are in the very front rank of their pro-
fessions. Our bankers, the Royal Bank of Queens-
land, Ltd., one of our best Queensland banking
institutions(through which shares can be obtained);
and the agents, one of the oldest-established firms
in Brisbane, would not countenance or assist
anything that was not absolutely legal or bona-fide.
The reason of the assistance of those mentioned
above in this undertaking is because they consider
that by its means many people will be settled on
these beautiful lands at small expense to themselves,
and thus helping what should be the great national
policy of Queensland, settlement on the lands.
Any shareholder who may be dissatisfied with
any of the arrangements of the distribution can
have his money returned upon application before
the drawing. The agreement as to titles for the
land, &c., can be inspected at the office of the
agents.
Register your letters and send 2 addressed and
stamped envelopes for reply and result. Drawing,
June 14th.
ARTHUR MARTIN & CO., LTD.,
Agents, Brisbane, Queensland.
