Newcastle Herald, 27th April, 1894.
Professor Plolxardl.
A BENEFIT ENTERTAINMENT!
In the Victoria Theatre last evening Professor
Richard gave, by arrangement with a committee
of citizens, an electrical entertainment in aid of
the charitable societies of the district. The house
was fairly filled in every part, and it is almost
unnecessary to state that the people were not
only interested, but at times excited, by what
they saw. In the unavoidable absence of the
Mayor, Alderman Moroney occupied the chair,
and on the stage were Mr. H. Berkeley, manager
of the entertainment; Mr, J. S. Rodgers, repre-
sentative of the Benevolent Society; Mr. T. H.
Raysmith, a representative of the Newcastle
Relief Society; and Mr. A. “umbury, of the
Mutual Help Society. These gentlemen formed
a committee, and, as a result of the entertainment,
the sum of £2O 4s. will be divided among the
benevolent societies named.
The Professor, in opening his lecture, explained
that it was his custom when he visited a large
town to give a donation to the charitable institu-
tions, and it was only right that he should do the
same in Newcastle, where, despite his critics, he
had made hosts of friends and done good busi-
ness. When he was asked to give a benefit enter-
tainment in aid of the charitable institutions of
this city, he gladly consented, and all the expenses
of the theatre, advertising, and other items were
paid by him. He had, of course, heard that the
hospital authorities refused to recognise the enter-
tainment or participate in the profit, * All he
could say on that subject was, if that institution
was so rich that it could afford to throw away a
considerable sum of money he was glad to hear it,
and he hoped the hospital would always be in
that flourishing condition. After referring to
electricity as a cure for certain diseases he called
for patients, and it was quickly seen that more
than the usual supply was forthcoming.
The first patient treated was Mr. Johns, who
was the bearer of a letter from the Mayor stating
that he had been afflicted with deafness for 20
years. When asked his name and address, the
man could not hear a word, although it was
bawled in his ear. After a course of treatment,
occupying 10 minutes, the man answered a series
of questions put to him in a moderate voice from
all distances up to four yards.
The second case was that of a youth, 14 years of
age, named Willian Atkin, who brought a letter
from the Rev. Seth Jones. The lad’s mother
accompanied him to the stage, and stated that he
had been partly deaf for seven years. The Pro-
fessor, after applying the current from himself to
the youth for five minutes, invited the committee
to examine the patient, who answered questions
put to him in an ordinary voice from the opposite
end of the stage. His mother unhesitatingly
affirmed that the lad had not heard so well for
seven years, and in questioning him herself, she
stated that she had never known him to hear so
well.
Bell, a resident of Newcastle for 22 years,
brought a certificate from the Mayor stating that
the man was blind, and four years ago he lost the
sight of both eyes. The Professor said that the
optic nerve of the right eye was dead, but there
was a chance that the other eye could be restored.
After five minutes’ treatment the man distin-
guished an apple from an orange, told the colour
correctly of two handkerchiefs, and described
several articles. He said that he could see the
audience, and without doubt he could see better
than he had done for years. In answer to the
committee the man said that he could only discern
light from darkness and distinguish some objects
with one eye, but he could at that moment see the
links in Alderman Moroney’s watch-chain. On
leaving the stage the Professor said that he would
give the man a course of treatment free of charge.
The next case was a very sad one, and there is
no doubt that the patient was stone blind. The
case was that of a girl, age 16. Alderman Robson,
the Mayor of Merewether, wrote, stating that the
girl had been blind for three years, and had been
treated by a number of medical men without the
slightest beneficial result. The Rev. E. W. Bris-
oombe wrote testifying that the girl had been in
the Sydney Blind Institution, and was totally
without sight. The Professor stated that the case
was almost hopeless, and if he succeeded in show-
ing her a glimmer of light it would be more than
he anticipated. After the treatment the poor girl
distinguished a lighted match held before her at a
distance of four feet, and she said she could see it.
Her mother assured the committee that her
daughter had never seen a glimmer of light for
two years. The Professor stated that he would
treat the poor girl free of charge during the
remainder of his stay in the city.
Mrs. Woods was the next patient, and she was
recommended by Mr. Alex. Thornton, J.P. The
woman is the mother of a large family, and for
several years has suffered from acute rheumatism,
especially in the legs. This case was also success-
fully treated, and the woman, who had to be
assisted to ascend the stage, left it by herself.
The last part of the entertainment consisted of
numerous experiments with the eleotrio current,
and for over an hour the Professor held nis
audience completely interested. At the conclusion
he was accorded a hearty vote of thanks on the
motion of the chairman, and a vote of thanks to
the gentlemen who managed the entertainment
brought the proceedings to a close.
Professor Richard will be in the city for another
week, when he leaves for the metropolis, where he
will make a second appearance before leaving foi
Europe.
Corresponclexice.
BE PROFESSOR RICHARD.
TO THE EDITOR.
Sir, —Will you allow me space in your columns
for the following remarks ? Professor Richard is
one of the most advanced medical electricians of
the present day. He has not come to the Austra-
lasian colonies to make a name for himself; he has
done that long ago in the old land. He has prac-
tised for many years in France, Great Britain,
United States of America, Spain, Italy, and other
European cities, and were you to examine the
documents he has in his possession, I think you
would arrive at the same conclusion. Four or five
months ago I consulted the best specialists in
Auckland, Christchurch, Sydney and Melbourne.
One and all came to this conclusion, that no mortal
skill could remove the obstruction to my sight,
and the only thing they attempted to do was to
order very strong spectacles. I determined, how-
ever, as a last resort, to seek the Professor’s advice.
I have travelled thousands of miles in order that I
might place myself under his treatment, and I am
to-day in a position to say publicly that the mark
is daily disappearing, and I expect in two or three
weeks to have ray sight restored. Is this not a
great triumph for electricity ? If any doubt the
veracity of these statements, I am to be found at
Mrs. Jones’, Watt and King streets, where they
can have ocular demonstration. —I am, etc.,
JAMES RAE, Financial Agent,
of Auckland, N.Z.
Newcastle, 25th April, 1894.
TO THE EDITOR 01 THE COLONIST.
Sir,—The name of Professor Richard is perfectly
familiar to me, he having lived in Park-lane. May-
fair, W., within a stone’s throw of where I resided
in London. The leading physicians there, acknow-
ledging the curative power of electricity, sent to
the Professor those of their patients requiring
electrical treatment: in fact, Professor Richard is
reputed to have made a fortune out of the London
medical profession. He is a man looked up to
and respected, and worked harmoniously with the
scientific and leading physicians of the day.
Yours, &c.,
F. Buxton, O.E.
Nelson, New Zealand,
February 27, 1894.
Newcastle Herald, Ist May, 1894,
Charlton-street, Newcastle,
April 30, 1894.
PROFESSOR RICHARD, M.E.
Sir, —Seeing you intend leaving the city in a
few days, I desire to convey to you my sincere
THANKS for your BENEFICIAL TREAT-
MENT of me in my sore affliction. Six years ago
I was attacked with rheumatic fever, which left
me a hopeless cripple. Have been twice in the
Newcastle Hospital; the first time left somewhat
improved, but have not been able to get about
without the use of crutches. After your first
treatment my back became stronger, and was thus
able to stand more erect and also to walk a short
distance without the aid of crutches. Have now
undergone five operations treated by you, and this
day am enabled to walk a considerable distance
without the aid of sticks. Although my limbs are
a bit bent, I am much stronger, and can now
place my feet flat on the ground. I feel myself
getting more useful every day, for which to you
my gratitude is due.
I hope, sir, you may be long spared to relieve
suffering humanity, as undoubtedly your treat-
ment of me has been very beneficial.
Yours respectfully,
George Millar

Lemons, imported from Messina, Italy, at
about sd. per dozen are extensively used by the
main-line of Queensland coastal steamers, and yet
last year thousands of luscious lemons lie rotting
in the Bowen district for want of a market.
Dear Bulletin.—The new-chum—or a
good many of him—is himself responsible for the
amenities which extract a wail from “Common-
wealth.” The creature that arrives here with a
drawl in its mouth, and pimply self-satisfaction
all over it, takes a long time to realise that, instead
of arousing respectful admiration, it is merely
regarded as a more or less deplorable ass. It is
this particular brand of idiot that makes so many
Australians assume every Britisher to be a pre-
tentious snob. Hiric the howl!— Old Chum.
A recent N.S. W. case indirectly calls to
mind a remarkable murder by a boy in a small
reefing district near Dunolly, Mt. Moliagul,
almost 30 years ago. The youngster, a lad of 13,
noted for his cheerful disposition, was so affected
by an intimacy that sprang up between his mother
and a miner living in an adjoining tent, that he
became morose, and spoke to nobody for three
weeks. One day he followed the miner into a
store on the camp, seized a large bacon-knife off
the counter, and drove it clean through the man’s
body, killing him on the spot.

In the S.M. Herald of April 24 appeared
a paragraph headed “An Incident of the V
bourne Boom,” which was duly credited to g
New Zealand Herald. The' par. in quest
originally appeared in The Bulletin, and tub
Fairfax organ could have had it three weeks
earlier by taking it direct and acknowledging it to
its true source—only its dignity blocked the way.
Bulletin pars are good enough for the Herald to
use, but as it only takes them by mistake at
second or third hand they are pretty stale by the
time the deluded penn’orth gets hold of them.
A new use has been found for “our
esteemed townsman,’’ that person so often men-
tioned by the country editor. Says the Mataura
(M.L.) Ensign : “The trap capsized. . . .
There were ten passengers on board, but most of
them saved themselves by falling on top of our
esteemed townsman, Mr. W. B. Anderson, who
sustained several severe contusions.” The Bul-
letin rejoices with an unholy joy at this incident,
and only regrets that these ten people didn’t
further save themselves by falling on the oldest
inhabitant as well.

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Herr Rassmussen, who is known throughout
the world as “The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” Was born in the little kingdom of Den-
;ijiark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. His parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
his life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, he left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
had quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; but he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
musseir succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
si on became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Alealine Remedies, suffice it to say
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depot, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in his celebrated work, “ The
Natural Doctor.”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist—his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies must have cured more human'suffering
in that perioff than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not hold any consultation
similar to a doctor'; his whole business es
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
He simply prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially for the nerves, and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
He prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, and advertises same, which
is the fairest way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one, containing all particulars
and many useful and valuable hints on health.
Herr Rassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and throughout the world. He is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold medalist, diplomee and Hon,
Corr. Mem.. of the Academic Parisienne des
Inventeurs, France ; last year he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold medal by several of his cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893 ; and in 1891 he issued his
“ Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages, ’
The First Rabbits In N.S. Wales.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
About 1861, I bought the well-known Maiden’s
hut and hotel, on the Murray, where Moama
now stands. Having been connected with Cobb
and Co., I started a coach to catch the
night mail at Bendigo, The late Mr. James
Maiden, most genial and experienced of squatters,
resided at Perricoota station, now the property of
Robertson, Wagner and Co., situated about 16
miles below Moama on the N.S.W. side of the
Murray. Maiden, having paid a business visit to
Bendigo, purchased from a seedsman there a case
of rabbits—seven does and a buck—which were
booked by ray coach, the old gentleman anticipat-
ing with glee the sport to be provided by a good
rabbit-warren like those preserved in Great
Britain. He spared no expense in making pre-
parations. Trees were felled and carted, and
every provision made for the comfort and safety
of the new purchase at a spot close to the beautiful
homestead and garden.
After great care on ray part the rabbits were
safely landed at Moama. I then drove to Perri-
coota and delivered the precious case that excited
so much curiosity on the journey. The rabbits at
once felt at heme, feed being plentiful among the
fallen scrub and trees; and woe betide any man
who kept a dog or introduced one on the run.
The rabbits “did splendidly,” as Mr. Maiden
said, approvingly, about a month after ; and, to
my surprise, in less than three months, he drove
up to Moama with a basketful of dead rabbits, the
result of an hour’s sport. He presented me with
three beauties, and I invited some friends to
supper; the attraction being boiled rabbits and
onions, the first ever tasted by any of us in Aus-
tralia. Shortly afterwards these game presents
became numerous, and a large hamper was sent to
me to be delivered in Bendigo to some of Mr.
Maiden’s friends, who appreciated them as some-
thing unprecedented. A long paragraph appeared
in the Bendigo papers reminding readers of
England and its luxuries.
Time went on, and before twelve months several
men and.horses had met with accidents, through
the burrows, 20 miles back on the run, for the
rabbits had multiplied beyond imagination.
Perricoota was then a cattle-station, and horses had
to be used for mustering; so the rabbits’ resorts
near the river were destroyed and thousands were
killed. Even then there was no acknowledgment
of the error made, because the rabbits were so
easily destroyed. At Mathoura station muzzled
ferrets were set to work, and, in five minutes, I
saw 21 rabbits caught. They were carried to
other parts. Instead of being proclaimed a
nuisance, young ones were kept as pets. Soon
after this they were seen at Deniliquin, and the
Edwards river being dry in parts they easily
crossed. They were afterwards seen at Moula-
mein, and thence dispersed themselves in all
directions towards the Murrumbidgee, and so
over the province. And that is how the rabbits
first came to New South Wales.

“ Bad as was the crime of Mr. M’Kay’s murder by the
Barraba assassins, their subsequent destruction of the

CLOSES THURSDAY, JUNE 14.—Every Competitor
allowed two guesses for each 1/- sent by Postal or P.O.
Order, 1/1 if in stamps. No coupons necessary. Add ex-
change to Postal notes other colonies. Total amount (less
10 per cent.) divided amongst successful guessers.
The Missing Word is deposited, in a sealed envelope,
with Mr. T. F. Hughes, the well-known Albury Bookseller,
and opened in his presence.
No. 7 Competition, which was of short duration, closed
with 922 guesses. The Missing Word was EARNEST, and
the sentence, when completed, read: —
“ Women are rapidly qualifying themselves for men’s
positions in life. If such a questionable change is desir-
able, what more earnest occupation could be found than
that at present monopolised by Ministers of the Gospel?”
The only successful guessers were—William Bolivar,
Swan-st., Richmond, Vic. ; Harriet Emily Kemp, Ascot
Vale, Essenden; and E. D. Llewellyn, Bairnsdale, Gipps-
land. Dividend, £6 18s. 4d. each. Predominating guesses,
eligible, congenial, apposite, ennobling, etc. Two without
any signature or address, stamped Christchurch, N.Z.
Burton A Metcalfe, Dean-street, ALBURY, N.S.W.

Adelaide senior football this season confined

Sydney Pony Association asks that the A. J.O.
will co-operate in reducing the amount of
But the horse is already stolen I It has long been
a blind man that even in prosperous times every
in the week is far more than the population can reMon-
ably stand. Why more people don t get into gaol under
the circs, is simply marvellous. Now, if there' werei a

would, of course, receive a severe shook, but it would only
be hastening the collapse which is certain if » h 6 P te ”j

upon gee-ges racing will stink—it stinks now as did

thoroughbreds will find their way to the Zoo.
Melbourne Football Club threatens to throw
open its gates to the public free of charge. It would do
much to purify the game if it carried out the bluff.
* * • •
Football in Melb. brings the people out like a
handful of sugar on an ant’s nest. To see the crowds last
Saturday springing from nowhere and trampling in
radiating lines over the grass-grown streets towards the
cricket-grounds would make a stranger imagine that the
bust-up city was booming again.

and Musgrove’s oricket-trophy won for him in so question-
Able a manner by a club*raate of his 5 and recommends
the abolition of the prize, which gives free admission for
two to the Princess Theatre for a year, on account of the
individualism and bad feeling it causes.

Dear Bulletin,— Re the alleged secresy of
“ Tattersall’s ” sweep drawings. In the May Day Cup
sweep run at Brisbane, two well-known Tamworth men
drew Dan O’Connell and Stafford, and were duly apprised
Of the fact by wire as usual. In less than an hour later
they each received wires from the respective owners,
requesting a “lay off.” Now, this isn't good enough,
and I think a warning note may induce “Tatt." to
stop the Brisbane leak wherever it is.—Yours, Mac.
* » • •
Dooley lays £5O to .£3O that he can beat Tut
Ryan in 20 rounds. He has a great advantage in height,
weight, and ring experience, but it is doubtful whether
he possesses the requisite fierceness. Five and a-half
years ago, just before his second fight with Slavin, he
failed in a somewhat similar contract with Billy M'Carthy.
Dooley is no better now, while Tut at present is superior
to what M'Carthy was then.
* * * »
Dear Bulletin,— Somebody tells you that Melb.
Grammar School is the centre of Victorian school cricket.
Not so. For years past they have been beaten by the
Scotch College, and several times by Wesley College.
The Scotch have an unbeaten record since ’B9, and have
just closed another season by gaining their usual 100 per
cent, average of wins. Sydney school cricket is certainly
bfiow Melbourne’s. —Jim Crow.

The Sketch says George Lohmann “has an
idea that Melbourne may be his ultimate borne.”
New* from the seat of war of the interproymcial
eight-oar race looks hopeful for N.S. Wales. Their catch
and finish is said to be better than that of their
opponents, and one of the best judges on the other side
says that although it will probably be a desperate race for
a mile, N.S.W. should about win.

Two well-known horses, Froude and Bright-
light, have won galloway races at a “pony ” meeting.
One of these days some of the speedy galloways may turn
the tables by winning Melb. Cup.

Ananias and Sapphire.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They were standing in a deep shadow
flnnsr by the wall of the old city graveyard. There
was a church not far off, but they had done with
pushed back his old felt hat and sur-
eutitiously wiped away two big beads of perspira-
tion. Sapphira kept rubbing the third finger of
0 of her little, hard-worked hands because there
something there not yet 10 minutes old. Now
d again she glanced up in a frightened way at
Ananias, and her lips trembled a bit. They were
beautiful lips, too, red and fresh and childish, not
parched and black as you might reasonably have
expected.
Bv-and-by Ananias pulled himself together.
“You needn’t be so ’fraid, Poll,” he said, but
his voice was not as steady as it might have been.
“What’ll we do now?”
“Ain’t we goin’ to have no ’oneymoon? she
said piteously.
He flung his arm round her waist and kissed
her in a burst of rough tenderness, for there were
nnlv a couple of cabmen about.
“ - Course we are, Mrs. Doolan, what d’yer say
to Robinson’s Beach —or Manly—that’s a fine
place, now—or how d’yer like Bondi ?—it’s only a
matter o’ tram-tickets to Bondi.”
But it was a country-bred Sapphira, and Sydney
was a vast and terrifying wilderness to her, s°
Ananias had the selection, and chose Bondi.
“Good Lor’, Polly,” he said, suddenly, in a
frightened whisper.
“What! Oh, Tom !”
“Don’t be a damn fool, Polly,” he said, ten-
derly, and stood upright and unconcerned as his
shaking knees would let him.
There was a policeman at the corner, gazing
thoughtfully in their direction, and the clergyman
who had married them less than half-an-hour
aero was coming down the street, on the other side
of the road ; seeing them, he crossed over.
“ Well, what are you going to do ? Are you in
any difficulty ?” ho asked pleasantly.
“No ” said Tom, “of course not!” He scowled
heavily and stuck his hands deep down in his
pockets. e y OU > r 0 going to take this nice little
wife of yours"for an outing away from these dirty
streets— this must always be a green day in her
memory, you know.”
Tn the church, at the altar-rails, it had not
seemed half so childish a Polly, nor half so boyish
■i Tom. and the clergyman looked from one to the
other in a troubled kind of way and wondered
vaguely if it was all right, after all.
“ Yes ” said Tom. “ Come along, Polly.
Polly’“came along.” She almost ran m her
trembling haste to keep up with Tom s
ranid strides, and the parson was soon left behind.
They walked up to Oxford-street through back
ways and short cuts as much as possible, and
caught a tram there for Bondi.
There was only one thought in poor, little
Sapphira’s head all that long day among the rocks
and limpid, beautiful pools that the crashing waves
disturbed from time to time.
“ Seventeen years and two months,” said the
angry waters as they broke and rushed headlong
up the wide beach. “ Seventeen years—and —two
months,” echoed the thin, timid ebb that ran back
into the wild, clamouring arms outstretched for it.
Two odd-looking rocks not far off made a distinct
17 and another one close beside was shaped like a
two,
Ananias had brought apples and fancy biscuits
and four custard-tarts, bub the hour-old wife
turned away from the wedding-feast with a pale,
miserable, little face and refused to be comforted.
“ It’s done now, Poll, old girl—don’t take on,”
he said, trying to mop up her tears with his shil-
ling silk handkerchief; “ the old people’s three
’undred miles off, and can’t go for us easy— wit’s
the ’arm, old chap, as long as we ain’t found out?”
But Sapphira had a pathetic little religion of
her own that had survived Sunday-school, and her
poor, soft heart was torn in two over her crime.
“ Your lie warn’t so big,” she sobbed, “you’re
gone twenty, and it ain’t m-much ter swear ter
Gawd yer twenty-one, bub I’m only seventeen,”
she buried her damp, little nose in the sky-blue
silk—“and two m-m-months.”
# * *
There was a-knocking at a big lawyer’s
door. They opened it and let in a woman to pri-
vate conference with the chief. Such a queer little
woman with white cheeks and goggle glasses and
an absurd bonnet, looking somehow like her own
daughter badly disguised and tryingtolookelderly.
Three minutes ticked away before her trembling
lips could form the question, “If a girl and hfr
feller sweared ter Gawd _in church they wus
twenty-one and got married, and if they wusn't
quite, what ’ud be like ter happen ter us?”
The chief smiled within his beard and looked
away from the rough little hands twisting to-
gether so nervously. The “us” gave Sapphira
away in one act.
“ It’s a false declaration, you know, in the first
place, and punishable as such,” he said.
“Oh Gawd, oh Gawd,” moaned the little
woman, “an’ what’ll ’appen ter us—l means
them ?”
“ Well, I suppose the man would be liable to
seven years for abduction, and both of them to a
sentence for making a false declaration,” said the
lawyer, “but the Judge might pass a light sen-
tence, thinking the young people were punished
enough by so early a marriage. ”
The little woman looked at him piteously over
the top of her-goggles. “But ’e mightn’t?” she
“Of course. In fact, it would depend on the
manner of Judge and the prosecution.’
The woman was at the door fumbling with the
brass knob. He opened it gently for her, and she
hastened blindly out. Then she came back and
poured some money on the table —ten shillings in
sixpences, threepennies, and coppers, and went
out again. Through the second door and down
the stairs, through the hall, and out into the
g^e g aspe( j ) hurrying along with
wild eyes, and dropping the goggles feebly on the
pavement.
Ananias followed, silent, terrified.
“It’s awfle—oh, Gawd, it’s awfie !’ she _ said,
stopping once and swallowing a terrible sob m her
“ Come on,” he said, pressing forward through
a maze of narrow, ill-smelling lanes and streets,
“Le’s ’ide.”
And they hid. Like outcasts who fear the light
and wanderers who ache for a rest they may nob
come to until the “ old people ” are six feet deep
as well as 300 miles away.
Bufe even when that time Ananias used to
scowl at clergymen, and Sapphira to water with
sorrowful tears the grave of her poor little buried
religion that she had never dared to use again.

“The open enmity of an absolute king is
less dangerous than the equivocal friendship of
constitutional John Bull.” —Heine.
It is said that the rival brewery lords,
Allsop Hind lip and Baas Burton, each drink the
other fellow’s ale.
Queen Victoria intends to knight the
Great Western Railway official who for 50 years has
personally superintended theroyal engmetoand fro
between London and Windsor. Nothing has ever
been known to go wrong with the tram under this
person’s management, which joyful fact is easily
explained. The line is kept clear for haif-an-hour
before and after Her Gracious begins to travel,
and a pilot-engine runs a mile ahead to guarantee
the safety of the rails. However, let this careful
functionary be promoted to the rank of Matthew
Davies, by all means.

Commiserating with a Sydney builder on the
slackness of trade, the writer recently received a shook.
“Not at all. 1 am building wooden and brick cottages
and sell them as fast as they are built. The fact is, I can
sell a new cottage as cheaply as most people can sell old
ones, and buyers, of course, prefer the new. Land is less
than one-half its former price ; bricks I buy at 30s. a 1000
where I used to pay £3, and I can get them laid at Bs.
instead of 14s. to 16s. as formerly ; everything else is pro-
portionally cheap. As to wooden cottages, lam building
four and paying £lO each for the wood-work. Tradesmen
are now ready to work at contracts by which they can
ram by long hours 30s. per week.”
♦ * * *
Certain recent stirrings of the muddy financial
waters reveal, among other things, that the practice of
“faking” bank accounts at balancing-day is a lot more
general than is usually imagined. The process is beauti-
fully simple. The men with vast and hopeless overdrafts
pay in cheques on other banks for immense sums- some-
times their own cheques and sometimes those of other
deadbeats—on the last day of the halt year. These are
credited to their accounts, and their overdrafts are
reduced accordingly ; and next day, which is the begin-
ning of another half-year, they draw out the money to
meet the aforesaid cheques, and the overdrafts go up to
th> ir original dimensions.
These cheques are often quite valueless, bub the
juggle makes the overdrafts look several hundreds of
thousands smaller, and the cash some hundreds of thou-
sands larger, than they really are, for an hour or two, and
misleads the shareholders accordingly. The bank gains
not one solitary copper by the transaction, and it
is done only tor purposes of misrepresentation and
deceit—in other words, it is, in its effect, a
gross fraud and a wilful falsehood. Only it
seems, in the present state of commercial morality,
to be practically impossible to sheet the fraud home to
anybody, for the board and ihe manager always profess
to have not. even the shadow of an idea why their most
hopeless debtors rush in and deposit immense cheques on
balancing day and draw them out again the day after.
The general impression seems to be that they do it for the
sake of a tittle healthy exercise on an empty stomach.

A miner writes The Bulletin that plenty good
alluvial is to be got in the Harden (N.S.VV.) district, and
many men could make good wages there were it not for
the prohibitive terms demanded by the land-owners.
Besides rent, some want 25 per cent., some even 50 per
cent., but most won’t let miners go on the land at all.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
If, according to B. R. Wise, the building
socielies which got the heads of so many N.S.W. citizens
gaol-cropped, had tailed after instead of before the banks,
“ I don’t think the directors would have been put on their
trial at all.” Can anything more serious be alleged
against the general administration of law in N.S. Wales?
It logically follows that the men sentenced to from three
to set en years’ hard labour have been martyrs to ignor-
ance or panic, or that there are men now helping the
Government to pass Bank Acts and otherwise sitting at
the head of public affairs who ought to be breaking stones
or nicking oakum in Darlinghurst Gaol.

Spurious florins flying about in Adelaide. If
silver falls much lower, the base metal will be worth as
much as the real article.

There is a flourish in Sydney papers over a few
local firms partly reinstating employes’ salaries—
retrenched so drastically over a year ago. But how
“ mum ” those papers were when the “ docking ” began 1
* * * *
Mr. Cork, London manager of the Commercial
Banking Co. of Sydney, lately read a paper on the
“Recent Banking Crisis in Australia,” before the
Institute of Bankers. Cork blames what he called
“the disgraceful cowardice" of the shareholders of the
English and Scottish and the London Chartered Banks
for all the trouble. Mr. Cork, of course, held a brief for
his own bank, and sought to differentiate between the
Commercial and other banks which were compelled to
reconstruct. This has given offence in some influential
quarters.
The disappointing part of Mr. Cork’s paper,
remarks The Bulletin’s London correspondent, is that he
offers no suggestions as to how a good state of affairs in
Australian banking is to be brought about. He told us
nothing new, and when he sat down none of us were a bit
wiser for all he had read. In this respect he is much
behind his protegfe, Mr. Peel.
* * * *
The Northern Territory paper relates the case
of a Chinaman who, some time ago, assigned his estate
paying his creditors 2d. in the £. It subsequently tran-
spired that he had at the same time carefully sent away
£4OOO to China.
* * • ♦
If Ooolgardie has done naught else it has given
a fillip to the fine art of prospectus-writing. A sample:
The leader of a proposed expedition is, we are told, an
expert bushman, because he has had a long career as
engineer and explorer on the Govt, railway lines of N.S.
Wales. “He will be supplied with astronomical instru-
ments to enable him to fix by solar observations ihe
course of the expedition, and guide it through the track-
less area under exploration.” The gentleman who drew
up this prospectus may understand navigating a ship,
but he does not seem aware that on land “ explorers ”
have to be content with stellar observations for latitude,
and work their longitude by dead reckoning. If the
promoters of this enterprise have found out a way of
carrying a chronometer safely overland without its going
out of order they have done more than anyone has before
them.
Also, as this doughty party intends to pass into
a vast tract of country “yet unpenetrated by white men,”
readers will be glad to hear that the services of an officer
recently of “The Prince of Wales Own ” (!) haye been
secured as “transport officer” (not transit commissioner,
mind you.) In conclusion: “It is confidently felt that
gold mines will be found that will surpass any discoveries
hitherto made.” If the party run out of everything
else they will at least have plenty of brass left.
* • • •
“A master butcher” complains in a letter to
Newcastle (N.S. W.) Herald that he can get no applications
in answer to repeated advts. for first-class butchers
although he pays top wages and his men dine at the same
table as himself. The butcher, take him all-round, is the
best organised, the best paid, most constantly employed
and the sleekest manual worker in Australia.
• * * *
One of the interesting things about the N.Z.
Loan and Mercantile business is the judicious manner in
which certain people in the inner circle got out and the
equally judicious manner in which certain other people
were left in. The people who got out wdre those who
talked loudest about the wonderful prosperity of the insti-
tution, and the people who were left in were those who
believed them. It was ever thus.

One biginstitution, which wentbrokeayearortwo
ago, is putting up a remai kable record. There are several
liquidators and ope of them has a son-in-law who is also
his partner. The son-in-law acts as agent to collect rents
and so forth for the defunct society, on the usual cominis-
sion. The society has been absolutely remorseless in
appointed trustee in his ( state. Most of the estates have
only yielded enough to pay legal expenses and the son-in-
law’s commission, so Ihe society doesn’t gain anything
worth mentioning, but it is a good thing for the son-in-
law. The society’s properties have been carefully
valued by the aforesaid son-in law, and various atti mpts
have been made, without much success, to dispose of some
of them hy auction. The son-in-law acted as auctioneer,
Altogether the son-in-law is having a good time of it.

Mr. Frederick Sargood announced, at the recent
meeting of the collapsed Freehold Investment and Bank-
ing Co. (Melh.), which institution it is proposed to re-
construct on an entirely new basis, that shares in the
reconstructed venture wouldn’t lie vvorih the paper they
were prinied on. The Bulletin demonstrated the same
thing several weeks ago. When a man owes £l, and lias
only 2s. to pay iL with, he doesn t improve things much
by issuing to himself scrip for the 18s. he is short, said
scrip being signed by hims-lf, and further guaranteed
by his own 5 per cent, debenture issued hy him-
self to himself, and deposited with himself as col-
lateral security. And that is just about what the
Freehold Investment Co.’s resurrection schi me amounts
to. The rest of the business at tin l meeting con-
sisted in a proposal to reduce the liquidators’ salarn s
from £B5O to £4OO a year each, whereli (ion the liquidators,
in effect, announced that they would sue the meet! g

An Australian G.F.O. .Savings Bank boasts a
depositor with a credit-balance of £14,000. By the rules
he draws interest on £2OO only, so that the Government
have charge of £13,800 for which they pay no interest.
This is funny, but the funniest part of it is that this
capitalist comes of the race who have made ibe term,
“shixty per shent.,” a household word. Another depositor
in the same bank has £7OOO there. The pur could surely
do better in 4 per cent. Govt, bonds, which execuiors
with trust money to invest will buy for cash - e-pecially if
you give them a commission of i per cent, for their ser.
vices in helping you to get coin for your debentures.

The “ Federal Institute of Accountants ” (what-
ever that may he), with an office in Melbourne,
is circularising the accountants of Austral a, re-
questing them to become members. An impressive
document sets forth that the object of the Institute is to
“ bring together all the duly-qualified accountants in the
different colonies to whom diplomas will be issued and
signed by the president and secretary.” The - fee is
£i Is., and the prospective member is anxiously requested
to “add exchange.” How the accountants can be
“ brought together ” in Melbourne, geejnr that they are
scattered all over Australasia, and what good the bringing
diploma vt'hicb, apparently, anybody can get who likes to
pay for it, are things b ft unexplained.

What Puzzled Balladune.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Strangers were uncommon in Bui lad tine, a small
township in Western Queensland, though. 9 nce
upon a time the residents had indulged in hopeful
visions of a railway, uhd locdmotiV'es-dragging
thither long trains of carriages'full hf opulenti»B-
- with much money to spend. Of course
this prospective railway was not to go,past
dune, which was to remain for ever the terminus,
and wax fat in consequence. But theso -fond
hopes were horn but to die young. Trade, some-
how, drifted: furtlmr away, and the coming rail-
way never came. Those who had speculatively
invested in town lots were unable to realise, and
Balladune settled down, discontentedly eriohgh,
SrillU'e Hi
to stagnation and a weekly coach. s: . “
Balladuuc, therefore, presented no attractions
for strangers to visit it. Attempts had indeed
been made, on more than one occasion, to institute
half-yearly races and a pastoral show, but they
came to nothing. The coach-fares, even- kept
away any poor players who might otherwise have
sought to rake in a scanty hay-crop in the. back-
blocks, and the arrival of the said coach was' the
one local distraction. ’ /
It was therefore with mingled surprise and
admiration that the inhabitants, who systematic-
ally turned out in force on these occasions; sdiw a
stout, pleasant-faced, well-dreSsed gentletnau
alight from the box-seat, ;, .
The coachman, upon being at opqe interviewed,
stated that be believed the passenger had GGme up
by train to Mount Bastion, the hated rival;town
which had cut them out with the railway; ‘and
that, so far as he was concerned, .he found’him a
thundering good so.t, and he,, wished: ho,always
had one of the same brand on the box with him.
Of the stranger’s business—he knew absolutely
nothing. ' i
It was generally felt that the coachman ; had
failed disgracefully in his duty, but as he,was too
important a personage to offend, nobody toldlhim
io. It was also considered that the setiiorcon-
stabie, who had charge of the peace elf the town-
ship, should keep a strict eye upon the stranger,
as in these days of levanting financial,
there was no knowing where ope might turn up.
When, however, the puundkeeper suggested ; :that
in that case a man of noticeable presence would
seek a crowded place where lie would not l.e an
object of popular interest, public qpjnipn- veered
somewhat, and it was agreed that ,(a : general
election being then pending) the strangerwas
going to run for a seat.
Meanwhile the gentleman under discussion,
who gave the hotel-keeper the somewhat ?well-
known name of John Smith, did nothing to
indicate a desire to represent the district or,-Other-
wise throw light upon the burning questidh of
the day. What had brought him to Balladune?
He chatted affably on the weather, the diet and
the fall in silver, but not one word escaped by
which he could bo “ placed.” In. appearance he
was equally non-committal. hay.e^been

Mr, Smith made but little use of the telegraph
during his stay. Possibly he laboured under the
absurd, though common, delusion that telegrams
in a small township soon become public property;
anyhow, he did not utilise the wire, but on the
return mail after his arrival despatched a bulky,
sealed epistle, duly registered, which, on being
eagerly scrutinised by the postmistress, was found
to bear the simply maddening address, “John
Smith, Esq., Box 009, G.P.0., Sydney.”
“I could have smacked his face,” said the dis-
appointed lady to an equally curious and dis-
appointed friend who agreed that “it was a shame
and should not be allowed ; for her part she did
not believe that there were two John Smiths in
Australia,” ... .
Meantime, as Mr. Smith paid his bill and simply
smoked, read and chatted about the season in a
most exemplary manner, the landlord of the Royal
Hotel resented any impertinent prying, fearing it
might annoy his guest and lose him that rare bird
of passage, a profitable customer. Mr. Smith,
therefore, was, or pretended to be, in ignorance of
the burning curiosity he had aroused in the minds
of the Balladune gossips.
11.
Fifteen miles from Balladune was one of the
few stations which assisted to prevent the utter
abandonment of that struggling township. It
was not a large and flourishing station with
artesian water, shearing-machines, a big overdraft,
and all the proper equipments of high-class
grazing. The owner had worked hard on it him-
self ever since the district was first settled. He
had experienced bad and good times, had suffered
from drought and flood, had hung on through all
with the grim pertinacity of his nature, and now,
in spite of the fallen value of stock and stations,
was still clear of debt.
He was a gaunt and grizzled man, by name
Hemmings. A man who neither spared himself
nor his men. The wandering swagman met with
but a snarling reception, and his dole of begrudged
rations was thrown to him with contempt. As
Hemmings’ neighbours were treated with little
more hospitality, the owner of Red Dyke was by
no means popular.
Still, in a certain way, he was much respected.
He dealt for his stores locally, and spent what
little he did spend in the district. He was
scrupulously honest, and, above all, his word, for
good or evil, was inviolable. So far as was known,
he had neither friends nor relations. Imagine,
then, the surprise of the inhabitants of Balladune
when he drove.into the township, pulled up at the
Royal, and demanded to see the mysterious Mr.
Smith. . , , , . ,
When the two met it was noticed that, although
Mr. Smith was beamingly genial in his greeting,
Hemmings did not reciprocate. But this was
according to his well-known disposition.
“ We cannot say all we have to say here, with-
out having some long-eared gaby on his knees at
the keyhole; you had better come out to my place
and stop the night,” said the visitor.
Mr. Smith nodded a cheery assent, then slowly
drew his finger and thumb round his throat, and,
with a waggish smile, remarked, “It is safe, I
presume?”
The other scowled silently and strode out to his
buggy. Smith went up to his room and in less
than a minute descended, joined him, and they
drove off.
Everyone who had the price of a drink on him
went into the bar to discuss the event, still mere
strange than even the coming of Mr. Smith him-
self.
“Mark my words,” said the poundkeeper, who
professed to have made a study of human nature,
“ there’s no love lost on the side of that Hem-
mings, and it licks me what a simple, affable chap
like Smith can have to do with him.”
“I’ve got it,” said the storekeeper, smiting the
counter. “That’s a deep cove, that Smith, for all
he s a good-natured one. He s a detective, that s
what he is, and he’s just gone out to Red Dyke
to take Hemmmgs quietly without any scandal.
When he went upstairs, ” he added, in impressive
tones, “ it was to slip the handcuffs in his pocket.”
Everyone finished his drink in solemn silence,
the idea was so novel and entrancing,
“Wonder what he’s done?” said one open-
mouthed lounger at last.
“ You may be bound,” went on the storekeeper,
encouraged by his success, “that it was done
years and years ago, most likely in some other
country.”
This was delightful. Why, it might be another
Deeming tracked down.
“He sent a note out to Red Dyke yesterday,
said the landlord.
“ And you never told us !” cried a reproachful
chorus.
“ Come,” said the poundkeeper, who had been
silenced for a while. “Ifhe is a detective which
J don’t say he ain’t, would he have sent out a note
to tell Hemmmgs he was here? ’
This was beyond the reasoning powers of the
conclave, and even the storekeeper left the ques-
t’oll unanswered,
The two men in the buggy were silent for some
time. After leaving the township the road passed
for some miles through a dark and barren scrub,
Smith uttered the first remark :
“ I was unexpected, I presume?”
“ I have never left off expecting you.”
“ I suppose if a body were deposited a mile or
two back in this scrub ”
“Before it was found it would be unrecogms-
able, and put down to some foot-traveller who had
lost himself and died of thirst.”
“Ah ! So much better than burying.”
“ Quite true,” replied the other, who seemed to
force himself by strong effort to answer coolly the
gibing remarks of his companion,
“ Burying is clumsy, but you have not come
here to discuss such questions ?”
“ I have not. I have come to claim the fulfil-
ment of your word. ’ . .
“After twenty years? ’ asked Hemmings, in a
firm tone.
“After twenty years, repeated Smith, in a
tone as h rm *
There was silence once more, and, in a short
while, the buggy emerged on a track of open
country, grateful to the eye after the close scrub,
“Supposing we defer further conversation until
we reach home—at least, what I now call home?
I will fulfil my word.”
The other nodded, and not a word more was
uttered until the station was reached.
There was little to recommend the place to any
one with an eye to comfort or beauty, for the
buildings were rude, and Hemmings, in his love
of loneliness, had built his own house at some
distance from the others ; but an expert would
at once have recognised the value of the grazing
country. Hemmings drove up to an outhouse and
they dismounted and walked up. When they
stood on the verandah, Smith, all whose geniality
had departed, turned and looked round.
“ You have been alone here for ?”
“I have been here fifteen years. Alone —for
thirteen.”
“Mrs.—ah—Hemmings then?”
“Lies buried there. The rough life and climate
killed her.”
“The property is unencumbered, I under-
stand ?”
“ The property, such as it is, is unencumbered.
Listen ! I have gone through such hardships on
this place, have fought against famine and the
elements so fiercely, knowing all the while that at
any time the summons from you might come;
that I tell you I love it, by the very memory of
my sufferings.” His voice, which had not faltered
when he spoke of the death of the woman who
should have been, but was not, his wife, trembled
at the remembrance of his years of toil.
“I give you your choice. You demand the
fulfilment of my vow. I will mortgage this place
to the hilt, and work it free once more with this ”
—and he held forth his brown and sinewy hand —
“ or take a change of clothes and a blanket and
leave you here, to wander forth in the world. I
ask you but one favour, Farrars, give me your
answer quickly.”
The other kept silence for a few moments; then,
as if to change the subject, said :
“My name is Smith, just now, as yours is now
Hemmings. I will give you my answer early
to-morrow morning. Meanwhile—not that I mis-
trust you, in spite of all—l may mention that
yesterday I posted to a safe hand a notification of
my presence here, and my motive in coming. It
will be read if a fortnight elapses without hearing
from me, so that, even if you deposit my body
in that convenient scrub, I shall soon be missed.”
The other waved his hand as though the idea
was too contemptuous for serious discussion ; then
he motioned his unwelcome guest inside and
showed him to a rudely-furnished bedroom. “My
accommodation is but limited,” he remarked,
“ but as you come of your own accord you must
be content with it.”
“I shall be satisfied, "returned Smith. “I have
fared worse since the smash.”
“ You are then ruined ? ”
“ Or I should not be here.”
“ I had thought better of you. If your coming
had been only inspired by the desire for just
vengeance I could have understood it; as it is I
must despise the feeling to which you confess.”
“I intend to combine business and —pleasure,”
was the calm reply.
It was midnight before Smith, who had retired
early, fell into a troubled sleep on his hard bed
almost immediately to awake again. Someone
moving in the house had aroused him. In spite
of his protestations of confidence his hand stole
under his pillow. He listened, and the sound he
heard made him arise noiselessly and steal on to
the verandah.
In the brilliant moonlight stood Hemmings
gazing out on the familiar scene, the rugged out-
lines of which not even the silver rays could soften
or beautify. But it was his. He had come there
when the land was a wilderness, had seen the
country alter and become fruitful under his care.
He had defied sickness, the brazen sky and the
devastating storm to dispossess him. It had been
to him a refuge from the past and his fellow men.
Smith watched the tall, lean figure shaken with
emotion, then he spoke sternly :
“Walters—for I will call you by your own
name—l came here only on an errand of vengeance,
to turn you forth naked. lam not a ruined man.
Let me speak,” for the other would have inter-
rupted. “ Nearly 20 years ago you stole my wife
from me. You made a fatal mistake. You
allowed her, or she did it without your knowledge
to take both money and jewels in her flight. I
could have cast you into prison branded as a thief.
But you know, and I admit, that in the encounter
betw’een us you spared my life. Then you swore
that at any future time when I demanded it you
would strip yourself of all your possessions and
give them up to me in tenfold payment of that of
which I had been robbed. I knew your character
knew you would keep the vow, although a poor
man when you made it, and I thought that it
would be sweet vengeance to come unexpectedly
upon you and demand fulfilment. I have come
and what have I found ? One sleeps beneath that
mound under a name she has no right to—dead, I
doubt not, of the remorse which would be stimu-
lated by your solitary life in this waste. You, the
other one, a younger man than I, look ten years
older. You have not a friend in the world; here
you will live and die without a companion but
those whose services you hire. lam amply
revenged. Keep your lonely acres out here. I
give you back your promise.”
He paused., The dark figure against the moon-
lit sky said nothing, but put its hands up to it*
face.
“To-morrow morning,” went on the other
quietly relapsing into his natural tone, “ send a
man to drive me into the township, for you and I
must never look on each other again.” He turned
and re-entered his room.
The Balladune people have never yet accounted
for the visit of Mr. Smith.

A well-known magistrate, recently de-
ceased, was in the habit of going upon a great
periodical “tear.” One night he and a friend
had a very copious drunk together, and as they
were yelling in the street after_ the festivity, a
policeman came along. Recognising the magis-
trate, he was about to sheer off, when that func-
tionary beckoned him with an unsteady hand, and
calmly gave his friend in charge for being drunk
and disorderly. Next morning the friend was
brought up before his late companion in intoxica-
tion, who sternly admonished him concerning the
inadvisability of making a beast of himself in
public places, and fined him £l. Then the
prisoner sent up a note to the bench : “Dear ,
you were drunk yourself. Isn’t =£l rather high.”
Promptly another note came back : “ Dear ,
you were caught; I wasn’t -£1 is very cheap
under the circumstances.”

Missing Word Competition-No. 14.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the
paragraph on the subjoined coupon—winch
coupon is to be cut out and forwarded to
•The Bulletin, accompanied by one shilling,
will be paid the total amount received in
response to this advertisement.
Only one guess is allowed on each Coupon,
but competitors may send as many Coupons
as they please.
Coupons received to Thursday, 31st
May, 1894.
The missing word is placed in a sealed
envelope in the custody of the manager of
the Bank of New Zealand, Sydney, and the
contents are known only to the manager of
this paper.
If the word is not named, the money will
be added to that of the succeeding com-
petition.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded
by post-office order or stamps of any pro-
vince,'; when the latter are sent, a penny
to be added for exchange.

Disunion mentis defeat. The N.S.W. Labour-members of Parliament, having tasted
the sweets of power, are naturally loth to relax their grip; while, just as naturally, many
jealous, aggressive and ambitious members of the Labour Electoral League Council, a seat on
which js the half-way stage to a Labour-metpbership, absolutely hunger for office, and act
accordingly. , Hence the present condition of the party. The Bulletin attri-
butes much of the present disunion, to the, fact that, in the Electoral League. Council are
many avowed candidates for Parliament,' who* in the ordinary course of human nature,
fight “ for their own hand.” As no, man can be a competent judge in his own cause, the
presence at Council Board of Parliamentary candidates is a fatal defect in the
Council’s constitution.
